B) Because the grant is for daily needs and easier to deduct from.
C) Because the stipend is just enough for daily needs, making deduction from it burdensome.
D) Because the killer prefers to pay from the grant.

Key Takeaway
When the 'āqila have both a subsistence stipend and a surplus grant, diyah is taken from the grant to prevent hardship.

The Killer's Inclusion in the 'Aqila: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Dispute on whether the killer is included in the 'aqila for paying diyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the killer's liability in diyah payment and evaluate their proofs.

Core Idea
The killer contributes to diyah as a member of the 'aqila, not because he is punished but because he is the doer.

This passage presents a classic inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the killer's role in paying diyah for unintentional homicide. Abu Hanifa holds that the killer is included among the 'aqila and must pay a share, while al-Shafi'i exempts him completely. The disagreement stems from different applications of qiyas (analogy) and the interpretation of the killer's excused status.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school (represented by al-Quduri) says about the killer's role in paying diyah. Note that the killer is considered one of the 'aqila and pays a share like any other member.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Determine which school opposes the Hanafi position and what their ruling is. Al-Shafi'i holds that nothing of the diyah is obligatory upon the killer.
This is a mukhtalaf fih issue between the two schools.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine al-Shafi'i's evidence. He uses an analogy (qiyas): just as the full diyah is not imposed on the killer (because he is excused), so too a part should not be imposed. The common factor (jami') is that the killer is excused due to mistake.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
See how the Hanafi school rebuts this. They argue that imposing the whole diyah would be oppressive, but imposing a part is not. Moreover, if the mistaken killer is excused, then the innocent (others) are even more excused, so the burden should not be shifted entirely to them.
The Hanafi response also invokes Quran 6:164 to support individual responsibility.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah) of this difference. In the Hanafi school, the killer contributes a share; in the Shafi'i school, the 'aqila bears the full diyah and the killer pays nothing.
This difference affects the distribution of liability in real cases of unintentional homicide.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وادخل القاتل مع العاقلة فيكون فيما يؤدى كاحدهم لانه هو الفاعل فلا معنى لاخراجه ومؤاخذة غيره وقال الشافعي لا يجب على القاتل شئى من الدية اعتباراً للجزء بالكل فى النفى عنه والجامع كونه معذورًا قلنا ايجاب الكل احجاف به ولا کذلک ایجاب الجزء ولو كان الخاطئى معذورا فالبرنى عنه اولى قال الله تعالى ولا تزر وازرة وزر اخرى

ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور عاقلہ کے ساتھ قاتل کو داخل کیا جائے گا تو قاتل اس دیت میں جس کو ادا کیا جائے گا عاقلہ میں سے ایک کے مثل ہو جائے گا اس لئے قاتل فاعل ہے تو اس کے اخراج کے اور اس کے غیر کو ماخوذ کرنے کے کوئی معنی نہیں ہیں اور شافعی نے فرمایا که قاتل پر دیت میں سے کچھ واجب نہ ہوگا جزء کو کل پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے قاتل سے کل کی نفی کے سلسلہ میں اور جامع قاتل کا معذور ہونا ہے تو ہم کہیں گے کہ کل دیت کو واجب کر دینا اس کو پریشانی میں مبتلا کرنا ہے اور جزء کا واجب کرنا ایسا نہیں ہے اور اگر خالی معذور ہے تو قتل سے برکی بدرجہ اولی ( معذور ہے ) ارشاد باری ہے اور کوئی دوسرے کا بوجھ نہ اٹھائے گا۔
He (al-Quduri) said: And the killer is included with the 'aqila, so he is in what is paid like one of them, because he is the doer, so there is no meaning to his exclusion and holding others responsible. Al-Shafi'i said: Nothing of the diyah is obligatory upon the killer, by analogy of the part to the whole in negating it from him, and the common factor is that the killer is excused. We say: Imposing the whole is unjust to him, and imposing the part is not like that. And if the mistaken (killer) is excused, then the innocent is more excused. Allah says: 'And no bearer of burdens shall bear the burden of another.' (Quran 6:164) Translation: Al-Quduri said: And the killer will be included with the 'aqila, so he will be like one of them in the diyah that is paid, because he is the doer, so there is no meaning to his exclusion and holding others liable. And al-Shafi'i said: Nothing of the diyah is obligatory upon the killer, by analogy of the part to the whole in negating it from him, and the common factor is that the killer is excused. So we say: Imposing the whole is oppressive to him, and imposing the part is not like that. And if the mistaken (killer) is excused, then the innocent is even more excused. The divine command: 'And no bearer of burdens shall bear the burden of another.'
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute between Hanafi and Shafi'i on whether the killer (qatil) himself is included in the 'aqila for paying diyah in cases of unintentional homicide. The Hanafi position, attributed to al-Quduri, states that the killer is included as one member among the 'aqila, so he contributes a share like everyone else. The reasoning: he is the direct perpetrator, so it would be illogical to exclude him and hold only others responsible. The Shafi'i position argues that no diyah is due from the killer at all, drawing an analogy from the fact that the full diyah is not imposed on him, so a part should also not be imposed; the common factor is that the killer is excused (due to mistake). The Hanafi response refutes this: imposing the whole diyah on the killer would be oppressive, but imposing a part (as one share among the 'aqila) is not. Furthermore, if the mistaken killer is excused, then the truly innocent (others) are even more deserving of being excused, but the Quranic verse 'No bearer shall bear another's burden' is invoked to argue that the killer should bear his own share, not that others bear it entirely. The Hanafi view thus maintains that the killer contributes a part, while the Shafi'i view exempts him completely.

Key Terms
عاقلہ ('aqila): The group liable to pay diyah on behalf of the killer, including the killer himself in Hanafi view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the killer's role in paying diyah?
A) He pays nothing because he is excused.
B) He pays the full diyah himself.
C) He is included as one member of the 'aqila and pays a share.
D) He pays only if the 'aqila cannot afford it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school includes the killer in the 'aqila for diyah payment, while the Shafi'i exempts him entirely, highlighting a fundamental difference in how analogy and individual responsibility are applied.

Exclusion of Women and Children from 'Āqila Liability
Rule with proof: women and children exempt from 'āqila diyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on women and children in the 'āqila and explain the rationale based on nusra and the proof from 'Umar's statement.

Core Idea
Women and children never share diyah because they lack nusra—support capability.

This passage clarifies a key boundary in 'āqila liability: even if a woman or child is registered in the military pension list (diwan), they are not obligated to contribute to diyah. The ruling is rooted in a statement of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab and the legal reasoning that diyah is tied to mutual support (nusra), which women and children do not provide. We will unpack the proof, the rationale, and the intra-Hanafi dispute with later scholars who allowed inclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the clear ruling: women and children, even if in the diwan, are not liable to pay diyah as part of the 'āqila.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof: the statement of 'Umar (RA) that 'a boy or woman does not pay diyah with the 'āqila.' Note the hadith reference and its authority.
This is a qawl al-sahabi, which Hanafis accept as binding proof.
3
Analyze the rationale (ta'lil)
Understand why: diyah is wajib on those capable of support (ahl al-nusra) due to their negligence in supervision. Women and children are not relied upon for support, so they are exempt.
The text makes an analogy to jizya not being imposed on them.
4
Apply the rule to the killer
Note that if the killer is a child or woman, they themselves pay nothing from diyah, unlike an adult male who pays a portion because he supports himself.
5
Resolve the intra-Hanafi conflict
Address the question about the later scholars who included women: the text clarifies that the original madhhab (zahir al-riwaya) is exclusion, and the later view is not the relied-upon position.
This affirms the hierarchy of sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

من الدية بخلاف الرجل لان وجوب جزء من الدية على القاتل باعتبار انه احد العواقل لانه ينصر نفسه وهذا لايوجد فيهما والفرض لهما من العطاء للمعونة لا للنصرة كفرض ازواج النبي عليه السلام ورضى الله عنهن. ترجمہ: اور اس طریقہ پر اگر قاتل بچہ یا عورت ہو تو ان دونوں پر دیت نہ ہوگی بخلاف مرد کے اس لئے کہ قاتل دیت کے جزء کا وجوب اس اعتبار سے ہے کہ قاتل عواقل میں سے ایک ہے اس لئے کہ قاتل اپنے نفس کی مدد کرتا یعنی امام ان کی معونت و مدد کرنا چاہتا ہے کیونکہ یہ بعض امور میں کچھ معین ہو سکتے ہیں۔ جیسے حضور اقدس صلى الله عليه وسلم کی ازواج مطہرات کو جو حصہ دیا جاتا تھا وہ نصرت کی بنیاد پر نہیں تھا بلکہ بناء پر معونت تھا۔ سوال یہاں یہ فرمایا گیا ہے کہ عورت عاقلہ کے ساتھ دیت میں شامل نہ ہوگی اور پہلے یہ مسئلہ گذرا ہے قال المتاخرون ان المرآة تدخل مع العاقلة الخ تو يه تعارض كيسان یہ کیسا؟ جواب وہ اصل روایت نہیں بلکہ بعض متاخرین کا قول ہے اور یہ اصل مذہب ہے۔
And there is no liability upon women and children among those who have a share in the diwan, due to 'Umar's statement: 'A boy or a woman does not pay diyah with the 'aqila.' And because diyah is obligatory only upon the people of support (ahl al-nusra) due to their leaving off of supervision, and people do not seek support from boys and women. For this reason, that which is a substitute for support, namely jizyah, is not imposed upon them. Explanation: If a woman or child's name is in the diwan for assistance, when diyah becomes obligatory on the diwan, it is not obligatory upon women and children, because the obligation of diyah is upon the people of support, and women and children are not among them, since they are not relied upon for help. For the same reason, the substitute for support (jizyah) is not obligatory upon them. If the killer is a child or a woman, they are not included in the 'aqila. Similarly, if a child or woman kills, there is no diyah upon them, unlike a man. This is because the obligation of a part of the diyah on the killer is based on him being one of the 'aqila, since he supports himself, but this quality is not found in them. Their stipend is for assistance, not for support, like the share of the wives of the Prophet (peace be upon him). Question: When women and children are not people of support, how can they be people of stipend? Answer: The stipend allocated for them is not on the basis of being people of support, but for assistance; the imam wants to assist them because they can help in some matters. For example, the share given to the pure wives was for assistance, not support. Question: Here it is stated that a woman is not included in the 'aqila for diyah, whereas earlier the ruling was mentioned from the later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) that a woman enters into the 'aqila. How is this contradiction resolved? Answer: That is not the original transmission; it is the statement of some later scholars. This is the original madhhab.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof covering the exclusion of women and children from the 'āqila (the group liable for diyah). It begins by stating the rule: even if women and children are registered in the diwan (military register), they do not pay diyah. The proof is a statement of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab (RA) that a boy or woman does not pay diyah with the 'āqila. The rationale is that diyah is obligation on 'ahl al-nusra' (those capable of military support), because they failed to supervise the killer. Women and children are not relied upon for mutual support, therefore they are exempt. The text then extends this to the case where the killer is a child or woman: they themselves pay nothing from diyah, unlike an adult male killer who pays a portion. This is because an adult male supports himself (yanṣuru nafsahu), a quality absent in women and children. Their stipends from the state are for assistance (mu'āwana), not for support (nusra), analogous to the wives of the Prophet receiving stipends. The passage answers two potential questions: (1) Why are women and children given stipends if not for support? Answer: For assistance. (2) How is this consistent with later scholars (muta'akhkhirūn) who include women in the 'āqila? Answer: That is a minority view; the original madhhab (as per the foundational texts) is that women are excluded. The passage ends with a clear affirmation of the original position.

Key Terms
دیوان (dīwān): The military register or pension list where names of soldiers and recipients of stipends are recorded.
نصرت (nuṣra): Mutual support, especially in military or defensive contexts. It is the basis for 'āqila liability.
جزیہ (jizya): A tax imposed on non-Muslim subjects in an Islamic state as a substitute for military service, since they are not required to fight.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence cited in the passage for excluding women and children from 'āqila diyah liability?
A) A verse from the Quran
B) A statement of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab
C) Consensus of the Companions
D) Analogy with zakat

Key Takeaway
Women and children are permanently exempt from 'āqila diyah liability because they are not people of support (nusra), even if registered in the diwan.

One City's 'Aqila Not Liable for Another City
Ruling with brief reasoning (ta'lil) on 'āqila liability across cities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the 'āqila of one city does not pay diyah for a resident of another city and explain the two bases (diwan and proximity) for tanāṣur that determine liability.

Core Idea
'Aqila liability follows tanāṣur: one's diwan or closest city pays, never a different city.

In Hanafi fiqh, diyah for accidental killing is paid by the 'āqila—the group of mutual supporters. This lesson clarifies a key boundary: the 'āqila of one city is not responsible for a resident of another city. The ruling hinges on tanāṣur (mutual support), which is established through the diwan (military register) or, if no diwan exists, through residential proximity. Understanding this helps determine which community bears the financial responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the explicit ruling
Find the sentence that states the ruling. The author says: 'ولا يعقل اهل مصر عن مصر اخر' — the people of one city do not pay diyah for people of another city.
2
Determine the basis of liability
The ruling is conditioned on tanāṣur. Identify the two possible bases for tanāṣur mentioned: through the diwan (military register) or through proximity in residence (qurb fī al-suknā).
3
Apply the diwan criterion
When each city has its own diwan, tanāṣur is through the diwan. Therefore, the 'āqila is the diwan of the person's own city—not any other city's residents.
4
Consider the alternative (proximity)
If tanāṣur were based on residential proximity (when no diwan exists), the people of the same city are still closer than those of another city, so the same conclusion holds.
5
Apply to a modern scenario
Today, the diwan might correspond to a community registry, tribe, or insurance pool. The principle remains: liability follows the group with which one has a tanāṣur relationship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يعقل اهل مصر عن مصر اخر يريد به انه اذا كان لاهل كل مصر ديوان على حدة لان التناصر بالديوان عند وجوده ولو كان باعتبار القرب في السكنى فاهل مصره اقرب اليه من اهل مصر اخر. ترجمہ: اور ایک شہر والے دوسرے شہر والوں کی طرف سے دیت نہیں دیں گے مصنف کی مراد یہ ہے کہ جب کہ ہر شہر والوں کے لئے علیحدہ دیوان ہو اس لئے کہ دیوان کے موجود ہونے کے وقت تناصر دیوان کے ذریعہ ہوتا ہے اور اگر تناصر سکنی کے اندر قرب کی وجہ سے ہو تو آدمی کے شہر والے اس کے زیادہ قریب ہیں دوسرے شہر والوں سے۔ تشریح: اگر ہر شہر کے فوجیوں کی کمپنی الگ الگ ہے تو جس کمپنی میں وہ ہے وہ کمپنی اس کی عاقلہ ہوگی اور وہی اس کی دیت ادا کرے گی۔ تو اس کو مصنف نے فرمایا کہ ایک شہر والے دوسرے شہر والوں کی طرف سے دیت ادا نہیں کریں گے۔ اور اگر ناصر کی بنیاد مسکنی کے اندر قربت کی وجہ سے ہو تو آدمی کے شہر والے دوسرے شہر والوں کے مقابلہ میں زیادہ قریب ہوں گے۔
And the people of one city will not pay diyah for the people of another city. The author means that when each city has a separate diwan, because mutual support (tanāṣur) is through the diwan when it exists. And if it were based on proximity in residence, then his fellow citizens are closer to him than those of another city. Explanation: If each city's soldiers have a separate company, then the company he is in is his 'āqila, and they will pay his diyah. Thus the author says that the people of one city will not pay diyah for the people of another city. And if the basis of support is proximity in residence, then the people of his own city are closer than those of another city.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with a brief reasoning (ta'lil) explaining that the 'āqila is determined either by the diwan (military register) or by geographic proximity. When each city has its own diwan, the people of one city are not responsible for the diyah of a person from another city; the liability rests on the diwan of the person's own city. Even if 'āqila were based on residential proximity (instead of diwan), one's fellow city-dwellers are still closer and thus the liability remains within the same city. The explanatory section (tashrih) clarifies that if each city has a separate military company, that company constitutes the 'āqila.

Key Terms
تناصر (tanāṣur): Mutual support, the basis for 'āqila liability. It can be through registration in the same diwan or through close residential proximity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man from City A is registered in City B's diwan, which 'āqila is responsible for his diyah?
A) City A
B) City B
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The 'āqila that pays diyah is determined by the diwan (or closest residential group if no diwan), not by the city of residence alone.

City Dwellers' Liability for Village Diya
Ruling on city dwellers paying diya for villagers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on city dwellers' liability for villagers' diya and explain the reasoning based on nusra and qurb.

Core Idea
City dwellers pay diya for their villagers due to mutual assistance and proximity, not mere lineage.

The Hanafi school extends the 'āqila beyond blood relatives to include place-based relationships. This passage from al-Hidayah establishes that when villagers cannot pay the diya for an accidental killing, the city they belong to becomes liable. The proof is based on the established practice of mutual help and the concept of nusra.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Identify the act (accidental killing in a village) and the ruling: city dwellers are liable when villagers are insufficient to pay diya.
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Locate the dalil
The author uses qiyas: villagers seek help from city dwellers, so city dwellers should bear their diya. The illa is nusra (mutual assistance) and qurb (proximity).
This is a ta'līl (reasoning) based on 'urf.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
How does the dalil prove the ruling? The 'urf of mutual assistance creates a responsibility. This is a Hanafi istihsan.
The proof is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalālah.
4
Note the cross-madhhab ikhtilāf
Other schools restrict 'āqila to 'asaba (agnates); Hanafi includes place-based affiliation.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.
5
Evaluate the tarjīh
The muftā bihi is the inclusion of city, based on the principle of nusra and the Zahir al-Riwāyah.
The ruling is wajib, not fard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويعقل اهل كل مصر من اهل سوادهم لانهم اتباع لاهل المصر فانهم اذا حزبهم امر استنصروا بهم فيعقلهم اهل المصر باعتبار معنى القرب والنصرة
ترجمہ: اور ہر شہر والے اپنے گاؤں والوں کی طرف سے دیت ادا کریں گے اس لئے کہ گاؤں والے شہر والوں کے تابع ہیں اس لئے کہ شہر والوں کو جب کوئی امر پیش آتا ہے تو گاؤں والوں سے مدد طلب کرتے ہیں تو اہل شہر ان کی دیت ادا کریں گے قرب اور نصرت کے معنی کا اعتبار کرتے ہوئے ۔ تشریح: اگر کسی گاؤں میں قتل خطا کا کیس ہوا اور گاؤں والے اداء دیت کے لئے ناکافی ہیں تو وہ شہر جس کے یہ گاؤں تابع ہے جوڑا جائے گا اس لئے کہ گاؤں والے شہر والوں کے تابع ہیں اور بوقت ضرورت اہل شہر گاؤں والوں سے مدد لیتے ہیں تو قرب اور نصرت کے معنی موجود ہیں اس لئے اہل شہر ان کی دیت ادا کریں گے
And the people of every city pay the blood money for the people of their villages, because they are followers of the people of the city. For when something befalls them, they seek help from them. So the people of the city pay their blood money based on the meaning of proximity and assistance. Translation: And every city dweller will pay the blood money on behalf of their villagers, because the villagers are subordinates of the city dwellers, because when something happens to the city dwellers, they seek help from the villagers, so the city dwellers pay their blood money considering the meaning of proximity and assistance. Explanation: If an accidental killing occurs in a village and the villagers are insufficient to pay the blood money, then the city to which this village is subordinate will be included, because the villagers are subordinates of the city dwellers, and in times of need the city dwellers seek help from the villagers, so the meanings of proximity and assistance exist, therefore the city dwellers will pay their blood money.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This is a ruling regarding the 'aqila (blood money paying group) in cases of accidental killing. It establishes that when a village is unable to pay the diya, the city to which it is affiliated becomes responsible. The reasoning (ta'lil) is based on the concepts of qurb (proximity) and nusra (assistance). Villagers are considered followers (atba') of city dwellers, and in times of need they seek help from the city. This principle prioritizes practical assistance over mere lineage, reflecting Hanafi istihsan. The ruling applies when the villagers are insufficient; otherwise, they pay themselves.

Key Terms
عاقلہ (‘āqila): The group responsible for paying blood money in accidental killings, here referring to the city dwellers for the villagers.
دیت (diya): Blood money paid to the heirs of a victim of accidental killing.
خطاء (khaṭā’): Accidental killing, which triggers diya obligation on the 'aqila.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Blood relation
B) Mutual assistance and proximity
C) Contract
D) Order of the ruler

Key Takeaway
City dwellers pay diya for villagers when the villagers are unable, based on the legal reasoning of nusra (mutual assistance) and qurb (proximity).

City Resident's 'Aqila: Ahl al-Diwan Regardless of Kinship
Intra-Hanafi dispute on 'aqila with qiyas talil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the 'aqila of a city resident whose blood relatives live in the village, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the condition of kinship.

Core Idea
For city residents, the 'aqila is the ahl al-diwan, not distant blood relatives, even without kinship.

Who pays blood money when a city resident with village relatives commits unintentional homicide? The classical Hanafi answer relies on the concept of ahl al-diwan – the military-administrative register of the city. This passage explores an internal disagreement: does the obligation of the ahl al-diwan require blood relation, or does their role as protectors of all residents suffice? The resolution uses a powerful qiyas analogy to guardianship transfer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State the Hanafi position: the 'aqila of a city resident are the ahl al-diwan of that city, regardless of whether they are blood relatives.
This is the position attributed to Imam Muhammad and considered sahih.
2
Pinpoint the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Some scholars interpret Imam Muhammad's statement as requiring kinship, citing his earlier remark 'the people of the desert are closer to him'.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
3
Analyze the opposing proof
The opposing view argues that obligation is based on closeness of kinship; since villagers are closer in blood, they should be the 'aqila unless the city dwellers are also kin.
The proof rests on the principle of wujub bi al-qarabah.
4
Understand the Hanafi response and qiyas
The text responds that even if kinship is the basis, the city dwellers are closer in location (makānan) and therefore have the ability (qudrah) to assist. This is analogized to the case of interrupted absence (ghaybah munqati'ah) in guardianship: when the closer guardian is absent, responsibility transfers to the more distant guardian who can act.
The analogy: villagers = closer guardian (absent due to distance), city ahl al-diwan = more distant guardian with actual capacity.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied‑upon position is that kinship is not a condition; the ahl al‑diwan always function as the 'aqila for city residents.
The text explicitly says 'it has been said that this is correct' (wa qīla inna hādhā ṣaḥīḥ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذين يذبون عن اهل المصر ويقومون بنصرتهم ويدفعون عنهم اهل الديوان من اهل المصر ولا يخصون به اهل العطاء

ترجمہ اور اہل شہر میں جس نے کوئی جنایت کی اور دیوان میں اس کے لئے کوئی عطیہ نہیں ہے اور باعتبار نسب گاؤں اس کے زیادہ قریب ہیں اور اس کا مسکن شہر ہے تو اس شہر کے اہل دیوان اس کی دیت ادا کریں گے اور محمد نے یہ شرط بیان نہیں کی کہ اس کے اور اہل دیوان کے درمیان کوئی قرابت ہو اور کہا گیا ہے کہ یہی صحیح ہے اس لئے کہ جو اہل شہر کی طرف سے دفاع کرتے ہیں اور ان کی نصرت کے لئے قائم ہوتے ہیں اور ان کی طرف سے مدافعت کرتے ہیں وہ اہل شہر میں سے اہل دیوان ہیں اور اہل دیوان مدافعت کے ساتھ اہل عطا ء کو خاص نہیں کرتے ۔

تشریح ایک آدمی شہر میں رہتا ہے اور اس کے نسبی اقرباء گاؤں میں رہتے ہیں اب اگر یہ شخص کوئی جنایت کرتا ہے تو جس شہر میں یہ ہے وہاں کے اہل دیوان اس کی دیت ادا کریں گے خواہ مجرم اور اہل دیوان کے درمیان کوئی قرابت ہو یا نہ ہو اور بعض حضرات نے اس کو صحیح قرار دیا ہے اس لئے کہ اہل شہر پر آنے والی مصیبت کی مدافعت وہ اس شہر کے اہل دیوان کا فریضہ ہے اور اہل دیوان فقط اپنی یا ابل عطا ہی کی نصرت کے ذمہ دار نہیں بلکہ تمام شہر کے لوگوں کی حفاظت و نصرت کے ذمہ دار ہیں تو اہل دیوان ہی مجرم کے اہل نصرت میں ہوں گے اور یہی عاقلہ ہو نگے ۔

یہ تفصیل اس وقت میں ہے جب کہ اہل دیوان اور اس مجرم کے درمیان کوئی قرابت نہ ہو اور اگر متن کی دوسری تاویل کی جائے کہ مجرم کے قریبی رشتہ دار گاؤں میں ہیں اور شہر کے اہل دیوان بھی اس کے رشتہ دار اور اعزہ ہیں مگر گاؤں والوں کے ساتھ قرابت کا قرب کے ہے اور اہل دیوان کے ساتھ قرابت کا بعد سے جب بھی شہر کے اہل دیوان بھی دیت ادا کریں گے۔ فرماتے ہیں :۔ ہے

مسئلہ کی مزید تفصیل

وقیل تأويله اذا كان قريبا لهم وفى الكتاب اشارة اليه حيث قال واهل البادية اقرب اليه من اهل المصر وهذا لان الوجوب عليهم بحكم القرابة واهل المصر اقرب منهم مكانا فكانت القدرة على النصرة لهم وصار نظير مسألة الغَيْبَة المنقطعة.

ترجمہ : اور کہا گیا ہے کہ اس کی تاویل یہ ہے کہ جب کہ وہ مجرم ان کا قریبی ہو اور کتاب میں اس کی جانب اشارہ ہے اس حیثیت سے کہ کتاب میں کہا ہے اور گاؤں والے شہر والوں کے مقابلہ میں اس کے زیادہ قریبی ہیں اور یہ اس لئے کہ ان پر وجوب قرابت کے حکم سے ہے اور شہر والے گاؤں والوں سے زیادہ قریب ہیں جگہ کے اعتبار سے تو نصرت کے اوپر قدرت انہی کو ہوگی اور یہ غیبت منقطعہ کے مسئلہ کی نظیر ہو گیا۔ تشریح بعض حضرات نے کہا ہے کہ امام محمد کے قول میں جو یہ فرمایا گیا ہے ” عقل عنه اهل الديوان من ذلك المصريال وقت ہے جب کہ مجرم اور ان اہل دیوان کے درمیان قرابت ہو چونکہ امام محمد کا قول جو اس سے پہلے گذرا ہے یعنی اهل البادية اقرب اليه " اس کی جانب مشیر ہے۔

اور اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ ان پر وجوب دیت قرابت کی وجہ سے ہے اور قرابت اہل مصر اور اہل بادی دونوں سے ہے اور گاؤں والوں سے قرابت کا قرب ہے لیکن اہل شہر کے ساتھ اگر چہ قرابت کا بعد ہے مگر تد بیر کا رب ہے تو جیسے کسی کے دو ولی ہوں ایک اقرب اور ایک ابعد اور ولی اقرب غیبت منقطعہ کے ساتھ غائب ہو جائے تو ولایت ولی ابعد کومل جائے گی جیسے بدایہ جلد ثانی میں اس کی تفصیل مذکور ہے اسی طرح یہاں گاؤں والوں اور شہر یوں کو اتار لیا جائے گا۔
Those who defend the people of the city and stand for their assistance and repel from them: the people of the register (ahl al-diwan) from the people of the city, and they do not exclusively assign this to the people of the stipend (ahl al-ata). Translation: And among the people of the city, whoever commits a crime and has no stipend in the register, and in terms of lineage the village is closer to him, and his residence is in the city, then the people of the register of that city will pay his blood money. And Muhammad did not mention the condition that there be kinship between him and the people of the register. And it has been said that this is correct, because those who defend on behalf of the people of the city and stand for their assistance and repel from them are the people of the register from the people of the city, and the people of the register do not exclusively assign assistance to the people of the stipend. Explanation: A person lives in the city and his lineage relatives live in the village. Now if this person commits a crime, then the people of the register of the city where he is will pay his blood money, whether there is kinship between the criminal and the people of the register or not. And some scholars have deemed this correct because repelling calamity that comes upon the people of the city is the duty of the people of the register of that city, and the people of the register are not responsible only for the assistance of themselves or the people of the stipend, but rather they are responsible for the protection and assistance of all the people of the city. So the people of the register are the criminal's people of assistance, and they are his 'aqila. This detail applies when there is no kinship between the people of the register and the criminal. And if a second interpretation of the text is adopted: that the criminal's close relatives are in the village, and the people of the register of the city are also his relatives and kin, but the kinship with the villagers is closer and with the people of the register is more distant, then still the people of the register of the city will pay the blood money. He says: Further detail of the issue: And it is said: its interpretation is that when he is related to them. And in the book there is an indication to this where he said: 'And the people of the desert are closer to him than the people of the city.' This is because obligation upon them is by virtue of kinship, and the people of the city are closer to him in place, so ability for assistance is with them, and it becomes analogous to the issue of the interrupted absence (al-ghaybah al-munqati'ah). Translation: And it is said that its interpretation is that when that criminal is a relative of theirs, and in the book there is an indication to this, in that the book says: 'And the people of the desert are closer to him than the people of the city.' And this is because obligation upon them is by the rule of kinship, and the people of the city are closer in terms of place, so the ability for assistance is with them, and this becomes an analogy to the issue of interrupted absence. Explanation: Some scholars have said that in the statement of Imam Muhammad wherein he said 'the people of the register of that city pay for him' it is when there is kinship between the criminal and those people of the register. Since Imam Muhammad's earlier statement, which is 'the people of the desert are closer to him', indicates this. And the reason for this is that the obligation of blood money upon them is due to kinship, and there is kinship with both the people of the city and the people of the desert. The kinship with the villagers is closer, but with the people of the city, although kinship is more distant, they have capacity for administration. So just as when a person has two guardians, one closer and one farther away, and the closer guardian becomes absent due to interrupted absence, then guardianship passes to the farther guardian, as detailed in Badaya volume two. Similarly here, the villagers and the city people are treated analogously.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the 'aqila (the group liable for blood money) of a person who lives in a city but whose lineage relatives live in a village. The ruling: the 'aqila are the ahl al-diwan (the military/administrative register) of the city, not the blood relatives in the village. The reasoning (talil) is that the ahl al-diwan are responsible for protecting all residents of the city, not just stipend-holders, so they naturally become the supporters ('aqila) of any resident. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani does not require kinship between the criminal and the ahl al-diwan; his view is deemed correct (sahih). However, some scholars interpret his statement to require kinship, using his own words 'ahl al-badiyah aqrab ilayhi' to argue that the obligation is based on kinship, but since the city dwellers are nearer in location, they have the ability to assist. This leads to an analogy with the issue of 'interrupted absence' (ghaybah munqati'ah) in guardianship: if a closer guardian disappears, the more distant one assumes responsibility. Thus, even if the city people are more distant in blood, they take over because the villagers are absent.

Key Terms
عاقلة (ʻāqila): The group of people liable to pay blood money (diya) on behalf of a killer who has committed unintentional homicide or similar.
أهل الديوان (ahl al-dīwān): The people registered in the military or administrative register of a city, who are responsible for defense and mutual support.
غيبة منقطعة (ghaybah munqaṭiʻah): An absence that is so complete that the person's whereabouts are unknown and communication is impossible, leading to legal effects like transfer of guardianship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a man lives in the city but all his close blood relatives are in the village, who is his 'aqila?
A) His village relatives
B) The ahl al-diwan of the city
C) Both equally
D) The state treasury

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that for a city resident, the 'aqila is the ahl al-diwan of the city, not the distant blood relatives, and this holds even without kinship, as confirmed by qiyas analogy to interrupted absence.

Temporary Resident's 'Aqila: Not Ahl al-Diwan
States the ruling for a temporary resident's 'aqila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a temporary resident's 'aqila are the city people and explain the condition of permanent residence.

Core Idea
A temporary visitor without a permanent home in the city is not supported by the city's 'aqila — residence matters, not presence.

In this lesson, we examine a specific ruling from the chapter on diyat: who bears the responsibility for blood money when a desert dweller commits a crime while staying in the city? The Hanafi position draws a clear line based on permanent residence. We will explore the text from Al-Hidayah and the analogy that reinforces the rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is a crime committed by a desert dweller lodging in the city without a permanent home. The ruling: the people of the city do not pay blood money (diyah) for him.
2
Identify the condition
The passage states that the city's ahl al-ata only assist those who have a 'maskan' (permanent residence) in the city. Without it, they are not liable.
Maskan means having a house or settled dwelling.
3
Analyze the analogy
The text draws an analogy: just as villagers do not pay for a city dweller who visits them, so too city dwellers do not pay for a visitor from the desert. The common illa is that the visitor is not a permanent member of the community.
The analogy reinforces the rule's symmetry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان البدوى نازلا في المصر لا مسكن له فيه لا يعقله اهل المصر لان اهل العطاء لا ينصرون من لا مسكن له فيه كما ان اهل البادية لا تعقل عن اهل المصر النازل فيهم لانه لا ينتصر بهم

ترجمہ اور اگر دیہاتی شہر میں آیا جس کا شہر میں کوئی مسکن نہیں تو اہل شہر اس کی دیت نہیں دیں گے اس لئے کہ اہل عطا ،اس کی مدد نہیں کرتے جس کا اس شہر میں کوئی مسکن نہیں ہے جیسے گاؤں والے اس شہری کی دیت نہیں دیں گے جو ان میں جائے اس لئے کہ شہری گاؤں والوں سے مدد نہیں مانگتا۔

تشریح اگرکوئی گاؤں کا ہی شہر میں آیاہواہے لیکن اسکا کوئی مسکن ارگھر وہاں ہیں تو کر اسے بنایت سرزد ہو جائے تواہل شہر آدمی اس کی جانب سے دیت ادا نہیں کریں گے کیونکہ جو شہر میں اہل عطاء ہیں وہ فقط شہر کے لوگوں کی مدد کرتے ہیں جیسے اگر گاؤں کوئی شہری جائے تو گاؤں والے اس کی دیت ادا نہیں کریں گے کیونکہ شہر والے گاؤں والوں سے مددنہیں چاہتے ۔
And if the desert dweller is lodging in the city, having no residence therein, the people of the city do not pay blood money for him, because the people of the stipend do not assist one who has no residence in it, just as the people of the desert do not pay blood money for the city dweller who lodges among them, because he does not seek assistance from them. Translation: And if a villager comes to the city and has no residence in the city, then the people of the city will not pay his blood money, because the people of the stipend do not help someone who has no residence in that city, just as the villagers will not pay the blood money of a city dweller who comes among them, because the city dweller does not seek help from them. Explanation: If a villager has come to the city but has no residence or house there, and a crime occurs from him, then the people of the city will not pay the blood money on his behalf, because the people of the stipend in the city only assist the people of the city. Just as if a city dweller goes to the village, the villagers will not pay his blood money because the city dweller does not seek help from the villagers.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage states the ruling for a temporary visitor (badawi nazil) who stays in a city without establishing permanent residence. If such a person commits a crime, the ahl al-diwan of the city are not his 'aqila and do not pay his blood money. The reason is that the ahl al-ata (stipend holders) only support those who have a permanent home (muskin) in the city. The ruling is symmetrical: just as a city visitor to a village is not supported by villagers, a village visitor to a city is not supported by city dwellers. This reinforces that the 'aqila relationship is tied to permanent residence and mutual support obligations, not transient presence.

Key Terms
نازل (nāzil): A person who is temporarily staying or lodging in a place without establishing permanent residence.
مسكن (maskan): Permanent residence; having a home in the city.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person from the village comes to the city and rents a room for a week. During his stay, he accidentally kills someone. According to the Hanafi school, who pays the blood money?
A) The city's ahl al-diwan
B) The village people (his original 'aqila)
C) The city's ahl al-ata
D) He pays himself

Key Takeaway
The city's 'aqila are only responsible for those who have a permanent residence (maskan); temporary visitors fall under their original community's responsibility.

Bedouin's 'Aqila When Temporarily in City
Ruling on bedouin's 'aqila when lacking permanent city residence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi condition of mutual support (tanasur) to determine which 'aqila is liable for blood money in cases of non-permanent residence.

Core Idea
Without permanent residence and mutual support, the city's 'aqila is not liable.

This lesson examines a specific ruling within the Hanafi law of blood money (diyah). The 'aqila system holds a group responsible for paying diyah in accidental homicides. But who is included in the 'aqila for someone who is not a permanent resident? The passage uses a clear analogy to show that transient persons are not covered by the local support network.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the specific case? A bedouin (بدوی) staying in the city without a permanent residence. The ruling: the city's ahl al-'ata' (people of stipends) do not pay his blood money.
The ruling is a negation of liability, not an affirmation.
2
Locate the reasoning (dalil)
The author gives a reason: 'because the ahl al-'ata' do not support someone who has no residence there.' This indicates that the basis of 'aqila is mutual support (tanasur).
The dalil is not an explicit text but a rational explanation.
3
Trace the qiyas (analogy)
The passage draws an analogy: just as desert people do not pay for a visiting city dweller (since he does not rely on them), similarly city people do not pay for a transient bedouin.
This is qiyas jaliy: the illah (effective cause) is lack of mutual support due to absence of permanent residence.
4
Apply the illah to determine the ruling
The illah is the absence of tanasur. Since the bedouin has no residence, he is not integrated into the city's support network. Therefore, the ruling of non-liability applies.
If the bedouin had a permanent residence, the illah would be absent, and liability would attach.
5
Note the cross-madhhab implication
This ruling is specific to the Hanafi school, which conditions 'aqila on tanasur. Other schools may assign liability based solely on locality.
Understand the basis of difference: other schools may not require mutual support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان البدوى نازلا في المصر لا مسكن له فيه لا يعقله اهل المصر لان اهل العطاء لا ينصرون من لا مسكن له فيه كما ان اهل البادية لا تعقل عن اهل المصر النازل فيهم لانه لا ينتصر بهم ترجمہ اور اگر دیہاتی شہر میں آیا جس کا شہر میں کوئی مسکن نہیں تو اہل شہر اس کی دیت نہیں دیں گے اس لئے کہ اہل عطا ،اس کی مدد نہیں کرتے جس کا اس شہر میں کوئی مسکن نہیں ہے جیسے گاؤں والے اس شہری کی دیت نہیں دیں گے جو ان میں جائے اس لئے کہ شہری گاؤں والوں سے مدد نہیں مانگتا۔ تشریح اگرکوئی گاؤں کا ہی شہر میں آیاہواہے لیکن اسکا کوئی مسکن ارگھر وہاں ہیں تو کر اسے بنایت سرزد ہو جائے تواہل شہر آدمی اس کی جانب سے دیت ادا نہیں کریں گے کیونکہ جو شہر میں اہل عطاء ہیں وہ فقط شہر کے لوگوں کی مدد کرتے ہیں جیسے اگر گاؤں کوئی شہری جائے تو گاؤں والے اس کی دیت ادا نہیں کریں گے کیونکہ شہر والے گاؤں والوں سے مددنہیں چاہتے ۔
And if the bedouin is dwelling in the city, having no residence there, the people of the city do not pay blood money for him, because the people of stipends do not support someone who has no residence there, just as the people of the desert do not pay blood money for a city dweller who dwells among them, because he does not seek their support. Translation: If a villager comes to the city and has no residence, the city people will not pay his blood money, because the people of stipends do not help someone without a residence, just as the villagers will not pay blood money for a city dweller who comes to them, because the city dweller does not ask for help from villagers. Explanation: If a village person comes to the city and has no residence or house there, then if he commits an offense, the city people will not pay blood money on his behalf, because the people of stipends in the city only help the city's own people, just as if a city dweller goes to a village, the villagers will not pay his blood money, because city people do not seek help from villagers.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on the 'aqila (clan responsible for blood money) for a bedouin (بدوی) who resides temporarily in a city without establishing a permanent home. The city's 'aqila (ahl al-'ata') is not obliged to pay blood money for him because the basis of 'aqila is mutual support (تناصر), and a transient person does not receive such support from the city's inhabitants. The proof is an analogy: just as the desert dwellers do not pay for a city dweller who only visits them (since he does not rely on them for support), similarly the city dwellers do not pay for a bedouin who merely resides there without settling. The commentary emphasizes that 'ahl al-'ata'' (people of the stipend register) only assist those who are permanent residents and are part of their support network.

Key Terms
عاقلہ (‘āqila): The clan or group responsible for paying blood money on behalf of a killer in cases of accidental or quasi-intentional homicide.
اہل العطاء (ahl al-‘aṭā’): The people of the stipend register; those who receive state stipends and are part of the organized military or administrative support system in the city.
تناصر (tanāṣur): Mutual support, assistance, and solidarity that forms the basis of the 'aqila system.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do the people of the city (ahl al-'ata') not pay blood money for a bedouin who dwells in the city but has no permanent residence?
A) Because the bedouin's tribe is obligated to pay.
B) Because the bedouin does not receive mutual support from the city's 'aqila.
C) Because the bedouin is not a Muslim.
D) Because the bedouin committed the crime intentionally.

Key Takeaway
The 'aqila liability in Hanafi fiqh is founded on mutual support (tanasur), which requires permanent residence and integration into the group; transient persons are not covered.

Dhimmi 'Aqila: Known vs Unknown
Ruling on dhimmi 'aqila liability for accidental homicide with qiyas proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on blood money for a dhimmi killer, distinguish when liability lies with the 'aqila vs the killer, and apply the qiyas to the case of Muslim merchants in dar al-harb.

Core Idea
A dhimmi's blood money falls on his 'aqila if known; otherwise on the killer, like Muslim merchants in enemy territory.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses the liability for accidental homicide (diyah) when the killer is a dhimmi. The key distinction is whether the dhimmi community has a recognized 'aqila (group that pays blood money) based on mutual support (tanasur). Understanding this ruling clarifies how Hanafi fiqh extends Islamic penal obligations to non-Muslim subjects while respecting their internal structures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and identify the two distinct cases: (1) dhimmis with a known 'aqila, and (2) dhimmis without a recognized 'aqila.
The ruling changes depending on the existence of the 'aqila.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For scenario 1, note that blood money is on the 'aqila like Muslims. For scenario 2, it is on the killer's own wealth.
The basis for treating like Muslims is that dhimmis accepted Islamic rulings in transactions and harm prevention.
3
Extract the illa (effective cause)
The presence of mutual support (tanasur) is the illa that transfers liability from the killer to the 'aqila. When tanasur is absent, liability remains on the killer.
Tanasur is the key concept behind the 'aqila system.
4
Analyze the qiyas
The passage analogizes the dhimmi without 'aqila to two Muslim merchants in dar al-harb: one kills the other. In that case, the 'aqila back home does not pay because they do not support the killer; thus the killer pays from his own wealth.
The asl (original case) is the merchant scenario; the far' (new case) is the dhimmi without 'aqila.
5
Derive the ruling
Apply the same illa (lack of tanasur) to derive that the dhimmi killer without 'aqila must pay blood money from his own wealth over three years.
The judge's decree starts the three-year period.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان كان لاهل الذمة عواقل معروفة يتعاقلون بها فقتل احدهم قتيلا فديته على عاقلته بمنزلة المسلم التزموا احكام الاسلام في المعاملات لا سيما في المعانى العاصمة عن الاضرار ومعنى التناصر موجود في حقهم ترجمہ اور اگر ذمیوں کے معروف عواقل ہوں جو دیت دیتے ہوں پس ان میں سے ایک نے کسی کو قتل کر دیا تو اس کی دیت قاتل کے عاقلہ پر ہوگی مسلمان کے درجہ میں اس لئے کہ ذمیوں نے معاملات کے اندر اسلام کے احکام کا التزام کیا ہے خصوصا ان معانی میں جو اضرار سے بچانے والے ہیں اور ناصر کے معنی ان کے حق میں موجود ہیں۔ تشریح ماقبل میں آیا تھا کہ اہل ذمہ میں قتل کا رواج نہیں ہے لیکن اگر ہو اور کسی ذمی کا جانا پہچانا عاقلہ موجود ہے جو دیت کا قتل کرتے ہیں تو اس صورت میں اگر ڈمیوں میں سے کسی نے کسی شخص کو قتل کر دیا تو اس کی دیت اس کے عاقلہ پر واجب ہوگی جیسے مسلمان قاتل ہوتا تو اس کے عاقلہ پر دیت واجب ہوتی اور معاملات کے اندر ذمیوں نے احکام اسلام کا التزام کیا ہے اور خصوصا وہ معاملات جو اضرار سے بچانے والے ہیں جیسے حد سرقہ اور حد قذف اور قصاص اور وجوب دبیت وغیرہ۔ اور با ہمی تناصر جس طرح مسلمان میں چلتا ہے وہ ان کے حق میں بھی موجود ہے۔ اگر ذمیوں کے عاقلہ مشہور نہ ہوں تو دیت قاتل کے مال سے قسط وار تین سالوں میں ہوگی وان لم تكن لهم عاقلة معروفة فالدية في ماله فى ثلاث سنين من يوم يقضى بها عليه كما في حق المسلم لما بينا ان الوجوب على القاتل وانما يتحول عنه الى العاقلة ان لو وجدت فاذا لم توجد بقيت عليه في ماله بمنزلة تاجرين مسلمين فى دار الحرب قتل احدهما صاحبه يقضى بالدية عليه في ماله لان اهل دار الاسلام لا يعقلون عنه وتمكنه من هذا القتل ليس بنصرتهم ترجمہ اور اگر ذمیوں کے لئے معروف عاقلہ نہ ہو تو دیت ذمی کے مال میں ہوگی تین سالوں کے اندر اس دن سے کہ اس پر دیت کا فیصلہ کیا گیا ہے۔ جیسے مسلمان کے حق میں اسی دلیل کی وجہ سے جو کہ ہم بیان کر چکے ہیں کہ وجوب قاتل پر ہے اور قاتل سے عاقلہ کی طرف تحول ہوتا ہے جب کہ عاقلہ موجود ہو پس جب عاقلہ موجود نہ ہو تو دیت اس پر باقی رہے گی اس کے مال میں دو مسلمان تاجروں کے مثل جو دونوں دار الحرب میں ہوں کہ ان میں اسے ایک دوسرے کو قتل کر دے تو قاتل پر اس کے مال میں دیت کا فیصلہ کیا جائے گا اس لئے کہ دارالاسلام والے اس کی طرف سے دیت نہیں دیں گے اور اس کا اس قتل پر قادر ہونا ان کی نصرت کی وجہ سے نہیں ہے۔ تشریح اگر ذمیوں کے لئے عاقلہ ہوں تو دیت ان کے عاقلہ پر ہوگی ورنہ خود قاتل کے مال میں ہوگی جو قسط وار تین سالوں میں وصول کی جائے گی اور تین سال اس وقت سے معتبر ہوں گے جب سے کہ قاضی نے وجوب دیت کا فیصلہ دیا ہے کیونکہ اصل وجوب تو قاتل پر ہوتا ہے پھر عاقلہ کی موجودگی میں وجوب عاقلہ کی جانب متحول ہو جاتا ہے اور اگر عاقلہ نہ ہو تو پھر وجوب قاتل پر برقرارر ہے گا جیسے اگر دو مسلمان تاجر دار الحرب میں گئے اور ایک نے دوسرے کو قتل کر دیا تو قاتل کے مال میں دیت واجب ہوگی اس کے عاقلہ پر نہ ہوگی ۔
And if the people of dhimmah have known 'aqilas (clans responsible for blood money) who regularly pay blood money, then if one of them kills someone, his blood money is upon his 'aqila like the Muslim, because they (dhimmis) have accepted the rulings of Islam in transactions, especially in those meanings that protect from harm, and the meaning of mutual support (tanasur) exists in their case. Translation: If dhimmis have well-known 'aqilas who pay blood money, then if one of them kills a person, his blood money is due on his 'aqila, just like if the killer were a Muslim, because dhimmis have accepted the rulings of Islam in transactions, especially those that prevent harm, like the hadd punishments for theft, qadhf, qisas, and blood money. And mutual support exists for them just as it does for Muslims. If dhimmis do not have a recognized 'aqila, then the blood money is from the killer's own wealth in three annual installments from the day it is decreed upon him, as in the case of a Muslim, based on what we have stated that the obligation is upon the killer, and it transfers to the 'aqila only when it exists; when it does not exist, it remains upon him in his wealth, like two Muslim merchants in dar al-harb, one of whom kills the other – blood money is decreed upon the killer in his wealth because the people of dar al-Islam do not pay blood money for him, and his ability to commit this killing is not due to their support. Explanation: If dhimmis have an 'aqila, the blood money is on their 'aqila; otherwise it is on the killer's own wealth, collected in installments over three years beginning from when the judge decrees the blood money liability, because the original obligation is on the killer, but when an 'aqila exists, the obligation transfers to them; if no 'aqila exists, the obligation remains on the killer, as in the case of two Muslim merchants who go to dar al-harb and one kills the other – the blood money is due on the killer's wealth, not on his 'aqila.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage addresses the 'aqila (blood money paying clan) of dhimmis (non-Muslim subjects of the Islamic state). It first considers the case where dhimmis have established organizations of mutual support ('aqila) that are known. When a dhimmi commits accidental homicide, his 'aqila is liable for blood money just like a Muslim's 'aqila, because dhimmis are bound by Islamic rulings concerning transactions and penal matters that protect against harm (e.g., hadd punishments, qisas, blood money). The basis is that mutual support (tanasur) exists among them. Second, if dhimmis have no recognized 'aqila or mutual support system, the blood money liability reverts to the killer's personal wealth, payable in installments over three years from the day of the judicial decree. The proof is an analogy: the original obligation is on the killer; it transfers to the 'aqila only if the 'aqila exists. This is similar to two Muslim merchants in dar al-harb (non-Muslim territory) where one kills the other: the Muslim 'aqila in dar al-Islam does not pay because there is no tanasur across the border, and the killer's ability was not due to their support. Thus, the killer pays from his own wealth.

Key Terms
ذمی (dhimmī): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state who is protected under the contract of dhimma, enjoying freedom of religion and obliged to pay jizya.
عاقلہ (‘āqila): The clan or group that pays blood money on behalf of the killer, based on mutual support (tanasur).
دار الحرب (dār al-ḥarb): Territory not under Islamic rule, where Islamic law does not apply and no protection agreement exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a dhimmi commits accidental homicide and has no recognized 'aqila, how is blood money paid?
A) It is waived because dhimmis are not subject to Islamic rulings.
B) It is paid by the state treasury (bayt al-mal).
C) It is paid by the killer from his own wealth over three years.
D) It is paid by the Muslim 'aqila of the city.

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi's blood money is on his 'aqila if known; otherwise on the killer's own wealth, based on the presence or absence of mutual support.

Cross-Religious 'Aqila Liability: The Basis of Mutual Support
Ruling on cross-religious 'aqila liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cross-religious 'aqila liability and identify the condition of open enmity that severs liability among non-Muslims.

Core Idea
Mutual support (tanasur) is the foundation of 'aqila; without it, no liability.

The 'aqila system assigns blood money payment to a group of relatives or supporters of the killer. But does this system work across religious boundaries? This passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies that mutual support is the essential condition. Muslims and non-Muslims do not support each other in that way, so they do not pay for one another. However, all non-Muslims are treated as one community for 'aqila—unless open enmity destroys that support.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two-fold ruling
First, state the rule between Muslims and non-Muslims: no mutual liability. Second, state the rule among non-Muslims: they are mutually liable as one millah, unless open enmity exists.
The ruling is conditional on the presence or absence of tanasur.
2
Locate the underlying principle
The foundational principle is tanasur (mutual support). The passage explicitly says liability is absent 'lādam al-tanāṣur' (due to lack of mutual support). Understand that 'aqila is not based on shared belief alone but on actual social solidarity.
This is a dalil 'aqli (reason-based proof) derived from the purpose of the 'aqila system.
3
Analyse the exception for open enmity
The basic rule (all non-Muslims as one millah) is qualified by the mashā'ikh and Abu Yusuf: if there is open enmity between groups (like Jews and Christians), liability ceases because tanasur is severed.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute resolved by the later scholars.
4
Identify the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position for fatwa is the qualification of open enmity. Even though the basic rule is from zahir al-riwayah, the later mashā'ikh and Abu Yusuf's view is given weight due to the change in social circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يعقل كافر عن مسلم ولا مسلم عن كافر لعدم التناصر والكفار يتعاقلون فيما بينهم وان اختلفت مللهم لان الكفر كله ملة واحدة قالوا هذا اذا لم تكن المعاداة فيما بينهم ظاهرة اما اذا كانت ظاهرة كاليهود والنصارى نبغى ان لا يتعـاقـلون بعضهم عن بعض وهكذا عن ابي يوسف لا نقطاع التناصر ترجمہ اور کوئی کافر مسلمان کی طرف سے اور نہ کوئی مسلمان کا فر کی طرف سے دیت نہیں دے گا تناصر نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے اور کفار آپس میں دیت دیں گے اگر چہ ان کی ملتیں مختلف ہوں اس لئے کہ کفر کل کا کل ملت واحدہ ہے مشائخ نے فرمایا ہے کہ یہ جو کہ ان کے درمیان ظاہری عداوت نہ ہو بہر حال جب کہ ظاہری عداوت ہو جیسے یہود اور نصاری تو مناسب یہ ہے کہ ان میں سے بعض بعض کی طرف سے دیت نہ دے اور ابو یوسف سے ایسے ہی منقول ہے تناصر کے منقطع ہونے کی وجہ ہے۔ تشریح چونکہ عاقلہ ہونے کا مدار با ہمی تناصر پر ہے اور مسلم و کافر کے درمیان تناصر نہیں ہے اس لئے مسلمان کافر کی اور کا فرمسلمان کی
And no non-Muslim pays blood money for a Muslim, nor any Muslim for a non-Muslim, due to lack of mutual support. But non-Muslims pay blood money among themselves, even if their religions differ, because disbelief is all one single faith (millah). The scholars (mashā'ikh) said: this is when there is no open enmity among them; but if open enmity exists, like between Jews and Christians, then it is preferable that some do not pay for others. And this is also transmitted from Abu Yusuf due to the severance of mutual support. Translation: No non-Muslim will pay blood money for a Muslim, nor any Muslim for a non-Muslim, because of lack of mutual support. Non-Muslims will pay blood money among themselves, even if their religions differ, because disbelief as a whole is one single faith. The scholars have stated: this is when there is no manifest enmity between them; but when enmity is manifest, like Jews and Christians, then it is appropriate that some do not pay blood money for others, and this is transmitted from Abu Yusuf because of the severance of mutual support. Explanation: Since the basis of 'aqila is mutual support, and there is no mutual support between Muslims and non-Muslims, therefore a Muslim does not pay for a non-Muslim, and a non-Muslim does not pay for a Muslim.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on cross-religious 'aqila liability. The key principle is that the 'aqila system is built on mutual support (tanasur), which does not exist between Muslims and non-Muslims. Therefore, a non-Muslim does not pay blood money for a Muslim, and a Muslim does not pay for a non-Muslim. However, among non-Muslims themselves, they do pay blood money for each other, even if they belong to different faiths (e.g., Jews, Christians, Zoroastrians), because all disbelief (kufr) is considered one millah (religious community) for the purposes of mutual responsibility. The text then qualifies this by citing the mashā'ikh (later scholars) and Abu Yusuf: if there is open enmity between two non-Muslim groups (such as between Jews and Christians), then the mutual support is severed, and they do not pay for each other. This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) based on the absence of tanasur in such cases.

Key Terms
کفر (kufr): Disbelief; all non-Muslims are considered as one single religious community (millah) for purposes of 'aqila and certain legal rulings.
تناصر (tanāṣur): Mutual support, the foundational basis for 'aqila liability.
ملت واحدہ (millatun wāḥidah): One single faith/religious community; used to unite all non-Muslims for 'aqila purposes despite different religions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When they are from the same religion.
B) When they have no recognized 'aqila.
C) When there is open enmity between them.
D) When the victim is a Muslim.

Key Takeaway
Mutual support (tanasur) is the linchpin of 'aqila liability; without it, no obligation exists, even among non-Muslims of different faiths.

Diyat When Dhimmi Aqilah is Unknown: The Killer's Responsibility
Ruling with qiyas analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyat for a dhimmi killer without known aqilah and explain the qiyas analogy that supports it.

Core Idea
Without known aqilah, diyat reverts to the killer's own wealth.

In Hanafi law, diyat for unintentional killing is normally on the aqilah (support group). But what if the aqilah is unknown? This lesson clarifies that the obligation shifts back to the killer, using an analogy to Muslim traders in Dar al-Harb to justify the reversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is unintentional killing by a dhimmi. The ruling: if aqilah is unknown, diyat is wajib on the killer's own wealth.
Note: This is a conditional ruling – if aqilah exists, it is on them.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a qiyas: the case of two Muslim traders in Dar al-Harb where one kills the other. In that case, diyat is on the killer's wealth.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The common illa (effective cause) is the absence of aqilah support. In Dar al-Harb, the Muslim community does not support the traders; similarly, the dhimmi has no known aqilah.
The text explicitly states: 'his ability to commit this killing is not due to their support.'
4
Identify practical consequences
The diyat is payable from the killer's wealth in three yearly installments, starting from the date of the judge's decree.

And if they (dhimmi) have no well-known aqilah, then the diyat (is payable) from his wealth in three years from the day it is decreed upon him, as in the case of a Muslim, because we have stated that the obligation is on the killer and it transfers from him to the aqilah if found; if not found, it remains on him in his wealth, analogous to two Muslim traders in Dar al-Harb, one of whom kills the other: diyat is decreed upon him from his wealth because the people of Dar al-Islam do not pay on his behalf, and his ability to commit this killing is not due to their support. Translation: And if the dhimmi have no known aqilah, then the diyat is in the dhimmi's wealth within three years from the day diyat is decreed upon him, as in the case of a Muslim, due to the reason we have explained that the obligation is on the killer and it transfers from him to the aqilah when the aqilah exists. So when the aqilah does not exist, the diyat remains on him in his wealth, like the example of two Muslim traders in Dar al-Harb, one of whom kills the other: diyat is decreed upon him from his wealth because the people of Dar al-Islam do not pay diyat on his behalf, and his ability to commit this killing is not due to their support. Explanation: If the dhimmi have an aqilah, diyat is on their aqilah; otherwise, it will be on the killer's own wealth, paid in installments over three years, and the three years are counted from when the judge decrees the obligation of diyat. Because the original obligation is on the killer, then with the existence of the aqilah, the obligation transfers to the aqilah, and if there is no aqilah, then the obligation remains on the killer, just as if two Muslim traders went to Dar al-Harb and one killed the other: diyat becomes obligatory on the killer's wealth, not on his aqilah.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the Hanafi school regarding the payment of diyat (blood money) for unintentional killing when the aqilah (assisting group) is unknown or absent. The text specifically addresses the case of a dhimmi (non-Muslim citizen of the Islamic state). The ruling establishes that if a dhimmi killer has a known aqilah, the diyat is on that aqilah. However, if the aqilah is unknown, the diyat becomes a debt on the killer's own wealth, payable in three yearly installments from the date of the judge's decree. The reasoning is that the primary obligation is on the killer himself; it only transfers to the aqilah if they exist. The text supports this with an analogy to two Muslim traders in Dar al-Harb (non-Muslim territory): if one kills the other, diyat is on the killer's wealth because the Muslim community in Dar al-Islam does not pay on his behalf, and his ability to kill is not due to their support. This analogy shows that the principle of aqilah responsibility depends on mutual support (tanasur), which is absent in that scenario. The commentary (sharh) in Urdu further clarifies the ruling and emphasizes that the three-year period starts from the judgment, not from the killing.

Key Terms
عاقلہ ('āqilah): The support group responsible for paying diyat on behalf of a killer who committed unintentional homicide.
دیت (diyah): Blood money or financial compensation paid to the heirs of a victim in cases of unintentional killing.
ذمی (dhimmī): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state who is protected under the dhimma contract.

Key Takeaway
When a dhimmi killer's aqilah is unknown, diyat is wajib on the killer's own wealth, paid in three yearly installments from the date of judgment, analogous to Muslim traders in Dar al-Harb.

Diyat Responsibility Between Muslims and Non-Muslims
Ruling on cross-religion aqilah with condition and exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyat responsibility between Muslims and non-Muslims, and identify the condition of open enmity that removes this responsibility.

Core Idea
Mutual support (tanāṣur) is the basis of aqilah; without it, diyat responsibility does not apply.

The aqilah system distributes diyat (blood money) among a person's supporters. But who is considered a supporter across religious lines? This passage from Al-Hidayah explains the Hanafi position: Muslims and non-Muslims do not pay for each other, while non-Muslims generally pay for each other — unless there is open enmity between their communities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two core rulings
Find the two distinct rulings: (1) No cross-religion diyat between Muslim and non-Muslim, (2) Non-Muslims pay for each other (with an exception).
2
Extract the legal basis (ʿillah)
The passage gives tanāṣur (mutual support) as the basis for aqilah. Note how it is used to justify both rulings.
Tanāṣur is missing between Muslim and non-Muslim, but present among non-Muslims (as all kufr is one millah).
3
Determine the exception
The general rule among non-Muslims is qualified: open enmity (muʿādāh ẓāhirah) severs tanāṣur and thus removes aqilah responsibility.
Example: Jews and Christians.
4
Note the intra-madhhab support
Imam Abu Yusuf is reported to agree with this exception, confirming the reliability of the ruling.
The text says 'wa hakaḏā ‘an Abī Yūsuf'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يعقل كافر عن مسلم ولا مسلم عن كافر لعدم التناصر والكفار يتعاقلون فيما بينهم وان اختلفت مللهم لان الكفر كله ملة واحدة قالوا هذا اذا لم تكن المعاداة فيما بينهم ظاهرة اما اذا كانت ظاهرة كاليهود والنصارى نبغى ان لا يتعـاقـلون بعضهم عن بعض وهكذا عن ابي يوسف لا نقطاع التناصر
ترجمہ اور کوئی کافر مسلمان کی طرف سے اور نہ کوئی مسلمان کافر کی طرف سے دیت نہیں دے گا تناصر نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے اور کفار آپس میں دیت دیں گے اگر چہ ان کی ملتیں مختلف ہوں اس لئے کہ کفر کل کا کل ملت واحدہ ہے مشائخ نے فرمایا ہے کہ یہ جو کہ ان کے درمیان ظاہری عداوت نہ ہو بہر حال جب کہ ظاہری عداوت ہو جیسے یہود اور نصاری تو مناسب یہ ہے کہ ان میں سے بعض بعض کی طرف سے دیت نہ دے اور ابو یوسف سے ایسے ہی منقول ہے تناصر کے منقطع ہونے کی وجہ ہے۔ تشریح چونکہ عاقلہ ہونے کا مدار باہمی تناصر پر ہے اور مسلم و کافر کے درمیان تناصر نہیں ہے اس لئے مسلمان کافر کی اور کافر مسلمان کی دیت نہیں دے گا۔ اور کفار اگر چہ باہم مختلف ملت رکھتے ہوں لیکن کفر سب برابر ہے اس لئے وہ دوسرے کی دیت دیں گے۔ لیکن یہ اس وقت ہے جب کہ ان میں کھلی ہوئی عداوت نہ ہو جیسے یہود و نصاری اگر چہ دونوں کافر ہیں لیکن آپس میں کھلی ہوئی دشمنی ہے اس لئے ان میں سے ایک دوسرے کی دیت ادا نہیں کریں گے کیونکہ باہمی تناصر جو عاقلہ ہونے کا مدار ہے منقطع ہے امام ابو یوسف سے بھی ایسے ہی منقول ہے۔
And a non-Muslim does not pay diyat for a Muslim, nor a Muslim for a non-Muslim, due to lack of mutual support (tanāṣur). And the non-Muslims pay diyat among themselves, even if their religious communities differ, because all disbelief (kufr) is one religious community (milla). They (the scholars) said: This is when there is no open enmity (mu‘ādāh) between them. But if there is open enmity, like the Jews and Christians, then it is befitting that they should not pay diyat for one another. And similarly it is reported from Abu Yusuf, due to the cessation of mutual support. Translation: And no non-Muslim will pay diyat on behalf of a Muslim, and no Muslim on behalf of a non-Muslim, due to the lack of mutual support. And non-Muslims will pay diyat among themselves, even if their religions differ, because all disbelief is one religious community. The mashā’ikh (leading scholars) have said that this is when there is no apparent enmity between them; otherwise, when there is apparent enmity, like the Jews and Christians, it is appropriate that they should not pay diyat for one another. And it is similarly transmitted from Abu Yusuf, due to the severance of mutual support. Explanation: Because the basis for being an aqilah is mutual support, and there is no mutual support between a Muslim and a non-Muslim, so a Muslim will not pay diyat for a non-Muslim, nor a non-Muslim for a Muslim. And non-Muslims, even if they have different religions, but all disbelief is equal, so they will pay diyat for each other. But this is when there is no open enmity between them, like the Jews and Christians: although both are disbelievers, there is open hostility between them, so one will not pay diyat for the other, because the mutual support that is the basis of aqilah is severed. It is similarly transmitted from Imam Abu Yusuf.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the rules of aqilah (diyat responsibility) across religious lines. The Hanafi school establishes two key principles: First, there is no mutual diyat responsibility between Muslims and non-Muslims because the foundation of aqilah is mutual support (tanasur), which does not exist between different faiths. Second, among non-Muslims themselves, they are mutually responsible for diyat even if they belong to different religious communities, because disbelief is considered one milla (religious community). However, an exception is made when there is open enmity (zahir al-adawah) between them, such as between Jews and Christians, in which case they do not pay diyat for each other because the mutual support is severed. Imam Abu Yusuf also transmitted this opinion. The Urdu commentary (sharh) reiterates that the basis of aqilah is mutual support, and contrasts the general rule with the exception for open enmity.

Key Terms
تناصر (tanāṣur): Mutual support and assistance, which is the basis for aqilah responsibility.
مِلَّة (millah): Religious community or creed. Here, all disbelief is considered one millah for the purpose of aqilah.
معاداة ظاہرہ (mu‘ādāh ẓāhirah): Open enmity or hostility that severs mutual support between groups.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis (ʿillah) for aqilah responsibility?
A) Blood relation
B) Shared residence
C) Mutual support (tanāṣur)
D) Same religion

Key Takeaway
Aqilah responsibility is built on mutual support (tanāṣur); without it, diyat is not owed across religious lines, and even within non-Muslim communities, open enmity cancels the obligation.

Diwan Transfer Affects Aqilah at Judgment
Intra-Hanafi dispute on diwan transfer and aqilah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on which aqilah pays diyat when a killer's diwan is transferred before judgment, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute with its practical consequence.

Core Idea
The aqilah at the time of judgment bears diyat, not at the time of crime.

This passage presents a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school about the timing of aqilah liability. The issue arises when a killer's diwan (military register) is transferred from one city to another after the crime but before the judicial decree. The Hanafi position, relying on the principle that the obligation to pay diyat crystallizes at judgment, holds that the aqilah of the new diwan is liable. Imam Zufar and, in one transmission, Imam Abu Yusuf argue that the aqilah of the original diwan should pay, as the crime is the obligating factor.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that according to Abu Hanifa and the relied-upon view, the aqilah at the time of judgment (Basra) pays.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Imam Zufar and one riwaya from Abu Yusuf hold the aqilah at the time of crime (Kufa) pays.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Follow the opponent’s proof
Understand their reasoning: the obligating factor is the crime itself, and at that time the aqilah was Kufa. They analogize to the case of transfer after judgment, where liability does not shift.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Note the Hanafi rebuttal: the obligation to pay wealth is only fixed at judgment, not at the time of the crime. Hence the aqilah at judgment is relevant.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: if a person moves his diwan before judgment, the new aqilah pays; if after judgment, the old aqilah pays by consensus.

And if the killer was from the people of Kufa and had an ‘atā’ (stipend) there, then his dīwān (register) was transferred to Basra, then the case was raised to the judge, then he (the judge) will decree the diyat on his ‘āqilah from the people of Basra. And Zufar said: He will decree it on his ‘āqilah from the people of Kufa, and it is a transmission (riwāyah) from Abu Yusuf, because the obligating factor is the injury (al-jināyah), and it has occurred, and his ‘āqilah at that time were the people of Kufa, and it becomes like the case where the transfer happened after the decree. Translation: And if the killer was from the people of Kufa and had ‘atā’ in Kufa, then his dīwān was transferred to Basra, then the case of killing was referred to the judge, then the judge will decree diyat on his ‘āqilah from the people of Basra. Zufar said that the judge will decree on his ‘āqilah from the people of Kufa, and this is one transmission from Abu Yusuf, because the obligating factor is the injury, and it has occurred, and his ‘āqilah were the people of Kufa, and it becomes like the case where the transfer happened after the decree. Explanation: A person is a resident of Kufa and is among the people of the dīwān in Kufa, and they are his ‘āqilah. If he kills someone by mistake, the diyat comes upon the people of the dīwān. But if before the decree, the killer was transferred to the dīwān of Basra, and after the transfer the case is presented to the judge, then the judge will decree diyat on the ‘āqilah. The question is: will the decree be on the people of the dīwān of Kufa or Basra? Our school (madhhab) is that the decree will be on the people of Basra. Imam Zufar and, in one transmission, Imam Abu Yusuf say that the decree will be on the people of Kufa. Their proof is that the original obligation of diyat is due to the crime, and when the crime was committed, his name was in the dīwān of Kufa, so the obligation is on them. Because if the judge had decreed diyat on Kufa and then after the decree he was transferred to Basra, then by agreement the obligation remains on Kufa. Therefore, it should be the same here. And our proof: that the wealth is obligatory at the time of judgment, because we stated that the obligatory (al-wājib) is the blood money (al-mithl), and by judgment it transfers to wealth. Similarly, the obligation on the killer, and his ‘āqilah bear it on his behalf. If that is so, then those who are his ‘āqilah at the time of judgment bear it, unlike the case after.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning whose aqilah is responsible for diyat when a person's diwan (military register) is transferred between cities after the crime but before the judgment. The Hanafi position (Imam Abu Hanifa and the relied-upon view) is that the aqilah at the time of judgment is responsible — in this case, the people of Basra. Imam Zufar and, in one transmission, Imam Abu Yusuf hold that the aqilah at the time of the crime (Kufa) is responsible, because the obligation arises from the crime, and transfer after judgment does not shift liability. The proof of the Hanafi view is that the actual obligation to pay wealth is fixed at the time of judgment, not at the time of the crime. The Urdu commentary explains the scenario and the reasoning of both sides, concluding with the Hanafi proof: the obligation of wealth (diyat) is only determined at judgment, and the aqilah at that time bears it.

Key Terms
دیوان (dīwān): A register or military department listing those entitled to stipends; in this context, the group of people who serve as the killer's aqilah.
عطاء (‘aṭā’): A stipend or salary paid from the state treasury to those registered in the dīwān.
قضاء (qaḍā’): The judicial decree or judgment of the qadi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this dispute, if a Kufi's diwan is transferred to Basra before judgment, whose aqilah pays diyat?
A) The aqilah of Kufa.
B) The aqilah of Basra.
C) The state treasury.
D) The killer's own wealth.

Key Takeaway
The diyat obligation crystallizes at the time of judgment, so the aqilah at that moment is responsible.
