Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
8 min
Timing of Diyah Obligation and Killer's Share
Ruling that diyah obligation is fixed at time of judgment, not earlier

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the 'aqila's obligation to pay diyah is fixed at the time of the judge's decree (qada) and apply it to scenarios involving change of residence or diwan.

Core Idea
Diyah obligation is not at crime but at judgment; transfer after judgment is invalid.

In Hanafi fiqh, the payment of blood money (diyah) in cases of accidental killing is not immediately due at the time of the crime. Instead, the obligation is established only when the judge issues his decree (qada). This lesson unpacks the rational proof for this ruling and its implications: the 'aqila (responsible clan) is determined at judgment, and if the killer later changes his registered residence (diwan), his own share is taken from his new stipend, not the old one. We also examine an intra-Hanafi dispute with Imam Zufar on whether the obligation can transfer after judgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
Read the source text and locate the core claim: the obligation of diyah is fixed by the judge's decree, not by the crime. Note the rational proof (dalil aqli) that transforms the equivalent (mithl) into monetary value and shifts liability from killer to 'aqila.
The proof hinges on the concept that qada is the cause (sabab) of the transfer.
2
Understand the 'aqila determination
The 'aqila that pays are those who are the killer's 'aqila at the time of judgment. If the killer moves, the obligation does not follow him.
The text gives an example: a killer's stipend is in Basra, so his 'aqila are Basrans.
3
Examine the killer's own share
The killer must contribute his portion of the diyah. This portion is taken from his future stipend ('ata), not past stipends.
Even if the killer's stipend changes location, the share is taken from the new stipend.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute with Imam Zufar
Imam Zufar analogized to a case where the 'aqila becomes insufficient, allowing addition of another tribe. He argued for transfer of obligation to the new 'aqila. The Hanafi response: transfer (naql) invalidates the first judgment, while addition (damm) strengthens it.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf): who pays if the killer moves after judgment.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a killer registered in Kufa diwan; judgment against Kufa 'aqila; then killer moves to Basra. State who pays and from which stipend the killer's share is taken.
The obligation remains on Kufa 'aqila; killer's share from future Basra stipend.

The Book of 'Aqila. [He said]: The judgment, because the obligation has been established by the judgment, so it does not transfer after that. However, the share of the killer is taken from his stipend in Basra, because it is taken from the stipend, and his stipend is in Basra. Translation: And our proof is that the wealth becomes obligatory at the time of judgment, due to the proof we have mentioned that the obligatory is the equivalent, and due to the judgment the obligation transfers to the wealth. Similarly, the obligation is upon the killer, and his 'aqila bears it on his behalf. When this is the case, at the time of judgment, whoever is his 'aqila will fulfill it. Unlike after the judgment, because the obligation has been settled by the judgment, so after that the obligation will not transfer. But the killer's share will be taken from his stipend that is in Basra, because blood money is taken from the stipend, and his stipend is in Basra. Commentary: This is our proof; its gist is that the obligation of wealth is not due to the original crime, nor does the equivalent become obligatory from the original crime. Also, the obligation of the equivalent is upon the killer, then the transfer from the equivalent to the value and from the killer to the 'aqila is due to the judge's judgment. So it is known that the obligation of wealth is due to the judgment. Therefore, at the time of judgment, those who are his 'aqila will have the blood money made obligatory upon them. And at that time, his 'aqila are the people of Basra, so the blood money will be obligatory upon them. As for the second issue upon which Imam Zufar analogized this issue, i.e., if the killer is still in the diwan of Kufa and the judge has decreed the blood money upon the people of the diwan of Kufa, then the killer is transferred to the diwan of Basra, now the obligation does not shift from those upon whom it fell, because that is transfer, and transfer is not permissible. Yes, in this it will be considered that the killer also gives the blood money as a member of the 'aqila. So the share of the blood money that will be collected from the killer will be collected from the stipend that is of Basra, and from the stipends that he previously received in Kufa, the blood money will not be collected, because blood money is taken from future stipends, and future stipends will be for him in Basra, not in Kufa.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage contains a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the timing of the obligation of diyah (blood money) in cases of accidental killing (khata'). The author establishes that the obligation for the 'aqila (the clan or group responsible for paying diyah) to pay does not arise at the moment of the crime but rather at the time of the judge's decree (qada). The proof is rational: the original crime only obligates the killer to pay the equivalent (mithl) of the victim, but the transformation of that equivalent into a monetary value and the shifting of that obligation from the killer to the 'aqila occurs through the judge's judgment. Therefore, the 'aqila that must pay are those who are the killer's 'aqila at the time of judgment. In the case at hand, the killer's stipend ('ata) is in Basra, but the judgment is made after he has moved, so his 'aqila are the people of Basra, and the killer's own share is taken from his future Basra stipend only. The passage then contrasts this with the view of Imam Zufar, who analogized to a scenario where the killer is in Kufa and judgment is given, then he moves to Basra. In that case, the obligation does not transfer because the judgment has already fixed it, but the killer's share is still taken from his new Basra stipend. Imam Zufar's analogy is rejected because it would invalidate the first judgment (naql), which is impermissible.

Key Terms
عاقلہ (‘āqila (pl. ‘awāqil)): The clan or group of relatives (usually paternal) who are collectively responsible for paying diyah in cases of accidental killing.
عطاء (‘aṭā’ (pl. a‘ṭiya)): A stipend or salary paid to soldiers or registered individuals from the state treasury (diwan).
قضاء (qaḍā’): The judicial decree or judgment of a qadi that establishes the obligation of diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) At the moment of the crime
B) At the time of the judge's decree (qada)
C) When the killer admits guilt
D) When the victim's heirs demand payment

Key Takeaway
The 'aqila's liability for diyah is determined at the judge's decree; subsequent changes do not transfer the obligation.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
6 min
Adding Tribes When 'Aqila Insufficient
Ruling that when 'aqila becomes insufficient after judgment, nearest tribes are added, not transferred

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the 'aqila becomes insufficient after judgment and distinguish between permissible addition (ḍamm) and prohibited transfer (naql).

Core Idea
When 'aqila is insufficient, add the nearest tribe—never transfer the obligation.

After a judge has decreed blood money (diyah) upon a specific 'aqila, what happens if that 'aqila becomes too few to pay? This lesson explores the Hanafi solution: adding the nearest tribe in lineage to the original group. This distinction between addition (ḍamm) and transfer (naql) preserves the finality of the judgment while ensuring the victim's right is fulfilled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal scenario
The passage deals with a situation where a judgment has already been passed against a particular 'aqila, but after the judgment, the 'aqila becomes insufficient (e.g., due to death or poverty) to pay the full diyah. Determine what the judge must do.
The judgment is already final; the issue is how to enforce it when the obligated group cannot pay.
2
Distinguish between naql and damm
Two possible actions: transfer the obligation entirely to another 'aqila (naql) or add additional groups to the original 'aqila (damm). Use the text to define each and identify the ruling for each.
Naql is prohibited; damm is permitted.
3
Analyze the rational proof (dalil aqli)
The proof is rational: naql invalidates the first judgment (ibtal al-hukm al-awwal) because it replaces the obligated party. Damm merely increases the bearers (taksir al-mutahammilin) while confirming the original judgment. Understand why this distinction is legally decisive.
The Hanafi school prioritizes the finality of judicial rulings (hukm) and avoids anything that would nullify them.
4
Apply the ruling to a concrete example
Consider a specific case: judgment was made on the tribe of Kufa, but they are insufficient. The judge adds the nearest tribe (e.g., Basra) to the original 'aqila, making them jointly liable. This is damm, not naql, because the original Kufa tribe remains obligated.
This example appears in the commentary of the passage.
5
Note the epistemological weight
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position. There is no intra-Hanafi disagreement reported. It is a settled principle in diyat law.
Authority weight: zahir_riwayah.

ترجمہ بخلاف اس صورت کے جب کہ عاقلہ پر فیصلہ کے بعد عاقلہ کم ہو جائے تو ان کی طرف نسب کے اعتبار سے اقرب القبائل کو ملا دیا جائے گا اس لئے کر نقل میں حکم اول کا ابطال ہے تو یہ کسی حال میں جائز نہیں ہے اور ملا دینے میں ان لوگوں کی تکثیر ہے جو اس کو اٹھانے والے ہیں جس کا ان پر فیصلہ کیا گیا ہے تو اس میں حکم اول کی تقریر ہے نہ کہ اس کا ابطال ۔ تشریح سوال ، آپ نے فرمایا کہ فقل جائز نہیں ہے لہذا اہل کوفہ پر فیصلہ کے بعد پھر اہل بصرہ پر فیصلہ وجوب دبیت کا نہ ہوگا ور نہ نقل لازم آئے گی اور اگر کسی کے عاقلہ پر دیت کا فیصلہ کر دیا جائے لیکن عاقلہ اتنا کم ہے جو ادائیگی دیت کے لئے ناکافی ہے تو پھر اس قبیلہ کو اس سے قریبی قبیلہ کے ساتھ ملا دیا جاتا ہے اور ان پر دیت واجب کر دی جاتی ہے حالانکہ یہ بھی نقل ہے کہ وجوب کسی پر ہوا ہے اور اس میں دوسرے لوگوں کو بھی داخل کر دیا گیا ہے ؟
جواب نقل نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ غم ہے اور نقلنا جائز ہے اور ضم جائز ہے کیونکہ قل میں حکم اول کا ابطال لازم آتا ہے اورضم میں حکم اول کا ابطال نہیں ہے بلکہ اس میں اور مضبوطی اور استحکام پیدا کرنا ہے اور حملین دیت کی تعداد کو بڑھانا ہے۔
Unlike the case when the 'aqila become few after judgment upon them, where the nearest tribes in lineage are added to them, because in transfer (naql) there is invalidation of the first judgment, so it is not permissible at all; and in addition (damm) there is increasing of the bearers of what was decreed upon them, so in it there is confirmation of the first judgment, not invalidation. Translation: Unlike the case when, after judgment upon the 'aqila, the 'aqila become few, then the nearest tribes in lineage will be added to them, because in transfer there is invalidation of the first judgment, so it is not permissible in any way; and in adding there is an increase of those who bear what was decreed upon them, so in it there is confirmation of the first judgment, not invalidation. Commentary: Question: You said that transfer is not permissible, so after judgment upon the people of Kufa, then judgment upon the people of Basra for the obligation of blood money will not occur, and transfer will not be necessary. But if judgment of blood money is made upon someone's 'aqila, but the 'aqila is so few that they are insufficient for payment of blood money, then that tribe is joined with the nearest tribe and blood money is made obligatory upon them, even though this is also a transfer, because the obligation was upon one group and now others are included? Answer: This is not transfer; rather it is addition (damm). Transfer is not permissible, but addition is permissible, because in transfer invalidation of the first judgment is necessary, and in addition there is no invalidation of the first judgment; rather in it there is strengthening and stabilization and increasing the number of bearers of blood money.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage addresses a nuance in the Hanafi law of 'aqila. After a judge has decreed diyah upon a particular 'aqila, if that 'aqila becomes too few (e.g., due to death or poverty) to pay the full blood money, the judge does not transfer the obligation to a different 'aqila (which would invalidate the first judgment). Instead, he adds the nearest tribe in lineage to the existing 'aqila. This addition (damm) is permissible because it preserves the original judgment and merely strengthens the group responsible. The commentary clarifies a potential confusion: why is adding not considered a form of transfer? The answer is that transfer would nullify the original ruling entirely, while addition merely supplements the existing obligors. This is a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh: the ruling once established must be upheld, but it can be supported by additional parties if needed.

Key Terms
نقل (naql): Transfer of the obligation from one 'aqila to another entirely, which is impermissible as it invalidates the original judgment.
ضم (ḍamm): Addition of other tribes to the original 'aqila to strengthen their ability to pay, without removing the original obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling when the 'aqila becomes insufficient after the judge's decree?
A) The obligation is transferred to a new 'aqila
B) The obligation is cancelled
C) The nearest tribes in lineage are added to the original 'aqila
D) The killer alone must pay the full diyah

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when the original 'aqila becomes insufficient after judgment, transfer (naql) is prohibited because it invalidates the judgment, but addition (damm) of the nearest tribe is required to strengthen the original obligation.

Effect of Change in Residence or Diwan Registration on 'Aqila Liability
Application of 'aqila determination rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the 'aqila is determined at the time of judgment, and determine whether a change in the killer's residence or diwan status before judgment shifts liability.

Core Idea
The 'aqila is fixed at judgment; changes before judgment shift liability, but not after.

In this lesson, we examine how the Hanafi school determines which 'aqila is liable for diyah when the killer changes his residence or registration status before the judge's decree. The key is whether the change occurs before or after the judgment. Through two illustrative cases — a resident of Kufa moving to Basra, and a bedouin joining the diwan — we see the principle consistently applied.

Guided Path
1
Identify the killer's initial status
Determine if the killer has a stipend (diwan) or not, and his original place of residence or tribe.
The initial status sets the baseline 'aqila.
2
Identify the timing of the change relative to the judgment
Did the change occur before or after the judge's decree? This is the decisive factor.
The judge's decree is the moment of obligation.
3
Apply the rule: obligation fixed at judgment
If before judgment, the 'aqila is the new group; if after, the original 'aqila remains liable.
Transfer (naql) of obligation after judgment is not permissible.
4
Consider the two specific scenarios
Apply the rule to the cases of a non-diwan resident moving to a new city, and a bedouin joining the diwan.
Both scenarios follow the same principle.
5
Confirm the ruling
State the final liability for each scenario clearly.

وكذا البدوى اذا الحق بالديوان بعد القتل قبل القضاء يقضى بالدية على اهل الديوان وبعد القضاء على عاقلته بالبادية لا يتحول عنهم
ترجمہ اور ایسے ہی دیہاتی جب کہ اس کو بعد قتل قضاء سے پہلے دیوان کے ساتھ لاحق کر دیا گیا ہو تو دیت کا فیصلہ اہل دیوان پر ہوگا اور اس کے گاؤں کے عاقلہ پر فیصلہ کرنے کے بعد وجوب ان سے ( گاؤں کے عاقلہ سے ) متحول نہ ہوگا۔
تشریح اس کے پیش نظر دوسرا جز سیہ پیش فرمارہے ہیں کہ ایک گاؤں کا شخص ہے جس نے خطاء کسی کو قتل کر دیا ابھی اس کے عاقلہ پر دیت کا فیصلہ نہیں ہوا تھا کہ اس کا نام فوج میں آگیا اب قاضی دیت کا فیصلہ کرتا ہے تو قاضی وجوب دیت کا فیصلہ اہل دیوان پر کرے گا اور اگر قاضی اس کے اہل دیوان میں ہونے سے پہلے اس کے گاؤں کے عاقلہ پر وجوب دیت کا فیصلہ کر چکا ہے تو اب وہ جنوب اہل دیوان پر نہ ہوگا اور نہ نقل لازم آئے گی ۔
And upon this, if the killer's residence is in Kufa and he has no stipend, and judgment was not passed against him until he settled in Basra, then judgment of blood money is given upon the people of Basra. But if judgment had been given upon the people of Kufa, it would not transfer from them. Translation: And upon this same principle, if the killer's domicile is in Kufa and he has no stipend, and judgment of blood money was not passed against him until he made Basra his home, then judgment of blood money will be upon the people of Basra. And if judgment of blood money had already been passed upon the people of Kufa, then the obligation will not transfer from them. Commentary: In light of the mentioned principle and proof, he presents a second particular: a person who is not among the people of stipend and is a resident of Kufa kills someone, but the judge had not yet decreed the obligation of blood money, and before that he made Basra his home. Now when the judge decrees the obligation of blood money, he will do so upon the people of Basra. And if the judge's decree regarding the obligation of blood money had already been upon the people of Kufa, then due to his transfer, this obligation will not change, otherwise transfer would be necessary, which is not permissible. Similarly, the bedouin, if he is joined to the diwan after the killing but before judgment, then judgment of blood money is given upon the people of the diwan. And after judgment upon his 'aqila in the desert, it does not transfer from them. Translation: And similarly, the bedouin, when he is joined to the diwan after the killing before the judgment, then judgment of blood money is given upon the people of the diwan. And after judgment upon his 'aqila in the desert, the obligation does not transfer from them. Commentary: In view of this, he presents another particular: a person from a village who accidentally killed someone, and the judgment of blood money upon his 'aqila had not yet occurred when his name appeared in the army. Now when the judge decrees the blood money, he will decree the obligation upon the people of the diwan. And if the judge had already decreed the obligation of blood money upon his village 'aqila before his joining the diwan, then that obligation will not be upon the people of the diwan, and no transfer will occur.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage applies the earlier principle to two specific scenarios. First, a killer who resides in Kufa without being registered in the diwan (no stipend) moves to Basra before the judge's decree. Since the obligation is fixed at the time of judgment, his 'aqila at that time are the people of Basra (his new residence). If, however, judgment had already been passed on the people of Kufa, the obligation does not transfer to Basra. Second, a bedouin (villager) who is not in the diwan kills someone, then before judgment he is enrolled in the diwan (army register). The judge then decrees blood money upon the people of the diwan (his new 'aqila). But if judgment was already passed on his desert 'aqila, it does not transfer to the diwan. In both cases, the key factor is whether the judgment preceded the change. The principle of no transfer (naql) after judgment is consistently applied.

Key Terms
مسكن (maskan): The place of residence or domicile of the killer, which determines the 'aqila for those not registered in the diwan.
دیوان (dīwān): The military register or treasury department where soldiers are enrolled and receive stipends; registration here makes a person part of the diwan's 'aqila.
بدوی (badawī): A desert-dweller or villager not registered in the diwan; his 'aqila is his desert tribe.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The obligation of diyah upon the 'aqila is fixed at the time of judgment; changes of residence or diwan status before judgment affect liability, but not after.

Joining a Weak 'Aqila with a Neighboring Tribe (Damm vs Naql)
Ruling on supplementing an insufficient 'aqila via damm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between damm and naql in the context of 'aqila and state the Hanafi ruling when the 'aqila is insufficient.

Core Idea
Damm strengthens the original obligation; naql nullifies it. Hanafis join a weak 'aqila with a neighboring tribe via damm, not naql.

This lesson addresses a practical scenario: the 'aqila (blood-money paying group) is too weak to pay full diyah. An objection arises that adding another tribe would transfer the obligation, invalidating the first judgment. We explore the Hanafi distinction between damm (joining) and naql (transfer) and why damm is permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The ruling applies when a judge has passed judgment of diyah on a specific 'aqila, but that 'aqila is too small or poor to pay. The judge considers adding the nearest neighboring tribe to share the liability.
The original 'aqila remains liable; the addition is supplementary.
2
Understand the objection
An opponent argues that adding a new tribe is a transfer (naql) of the obligation, which would nullify the original judgment against the first 'aqila. This would be impermissible if it invalidates the first judgment.
Naql implies complete removal of the original duty.
3
Distinguish damm from naql
The Hanafi response clarifies that this is damm (joining), not naql. In damm, the original obligation remains and is strengthened by adding more bearers. In naql, the original obligation is abolished and replaced.
Damm increases the number of responsible parties without voiding the first judgment.
4
Apply the Hanafi position
The judge attaches the neighboring tribe to the original 'aqila, making both jointly liable for the diyah. The original judgment stands, and the new tribe is added as an additional bearer.
This is based on istihsan and the principle of facilitating payment.
5
Recognize the underlying principle
The ruling reflects a broader Hanafi principle: obligations should be fulfilled with ease, and procedural adjustments that do not void prior judgments are permissible. Damm preserves the original ruling while expanding the pool of payers.
This avoids injustice to the victim's family while respecting the original judgment.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لازم آئے گی اور اگر کسی کے عاقلہ پر دیت کا فیصلہ کر دیا جائے لیکن عاقلہ اتنا کم ہے جو ادائیگی دیت کے لئے ناکافی ہے تو پھر اس قبیلہ کو اس سے قریبی قبیلہ کے ساتھ ملا دیا جاتا ہے اور ان پر دیت واجب کر دی جاتی ہے حالانکہ یہ بھی نقل ہے کہ وجوب کسی پر ہوا ہے اور اس میں دوسرے لوگوں کو بھی داخل کر دیا گیا ہے ؟ جواب نقل نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ [غم؟] ہے اور [نقلنا؟] جائز ہے اور ضم جائز ہے کیونکہ [قل؟] میں حکم اول کا ابطال لازم آتا ہے اورضم میں حکم اول کا ابطال نہیں ہے بلکہ اس میں اور مضبوطی اور استحکام پیدا کرنا ہے اور حملین دیت کی تعداد کو بڑھانا ہے۔
It will be necessary, and if judgment of diyah is passed on someone's 'aqila but the 'aqila is so little that it is insufficient for payment of diyah, then that tribe is joined with the nearest tribe and the diyah is made obligatory on them. Even though it is said this is a transfer (naql) of obligation from one group to another, and that other people are included. Answer: It is not a transfer, rather it is [damm?], and [naql?] is permissible? and damm is permissible, because in [naql?] nullification of the first judgment is necessary, while in damm there is no nullification of the first judgment; rather it strengthens and consolidates it, and increases the number of bearers of the diyah.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling and a response to an objection regarding the 'aqila's liability for diyah. The ruling states that if the 'aqila (the group responsible for paying blood-money) is too poor to pay the full diyah, the nearest neighboring tribe is joined to them, and both become jointly liable. The opponent objects that this amounts to a transfer (naql) of the obligation from the original 'aqila to a new group, which would invalidate the first judgment. The Hanafi response explains that this is not a transfer but a joining (damm). In a transfer, the original obligation is completely removed and placed elsewhere, nullifying the first judgment. In a joining, the original obligation remains and is strengthened by adding additional bearers. Thus, the first judgment is not nullified but rather reinforced. This distinction is a key principle in Hanafi fiqh when dealing with collective responsibility for diyah. The term 'aqila refers to the male agnates of the killer who bear the diyah for unintentional homicide. This ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) and the principle of ease in fulfilling obligations.

Key Terms
عاقلہ ('aqila): The group of male agnates of the killer who are responsible for paying diyah for unintentional homicide.
ضم (damm): Joining an additional group to the original 'aqila to strengthen the obligation, without nullifying the original judgment.
نقل (naql): Transfer of an obligation from one party to another, which would nullify the original judgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The original obligation is transferred to the new tribe
B) The original obligation is voided and replaced
C) The original obligation is strengthened by adding more bearers
D) The original tribe is exempted entirely

Key Takeaway
Hanafis distinguish damm from naql: damm adds bearers without voiding the original judgment, allowing an insufficient 'aqila to be supplemented by a neighboring tribe.

Transfer of ‘Aqila Liability Before and After Qadi’s Judgment
Two examples illustrating when liability transfers with change of residence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on transfer of ‘aqila liability to scenarios involving change of residence or military registration, distinguishing between before and after the qadi’s judgment.

Core Idea
The ‘aqila liable for diyah is fixed at the time of the qadi’s judgment; changes before shift liability, after it is locked.

The obligation of diyah falls on the ‘aqila (the killer's helper group) at the moment the qadi pronounces judgment. But what if the killer changes residence or status before that moment? This lesson presents two case studies from Hanafi fiqh that clarify when liability transfers and when it remains fixed. Understanding this timing is crucial for correctly assigning diyah obligations in cases of unintentional homicide.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
The obligation of diyah on the ‘aqila becomes fixed at the moment of the qadi’s judgment. Any change in the killer’s residence or status (e.g., joining the diwan) before that moment will change which group is liable.
The underlying wisdom (istihsan) is that the ‘aqila that can pay most easily—such as those receiving ‘ata—should bear the burden.
2
Apply to the first example: change of residence
A Kufan without ‘ata kills a person and moves to Basra before any judgment. The qadi will pass judgment on the people of Basra (the new ‘aqila). If the qadi had already judged on the Kufans, the obligation does not transfer.
The judgment ‘locks in’ the liable group.
3
Apply to the second example: enrollment in the diwan
A bedouin (villager) kills someone and then, before judgment, is enrolled in the diwan (military register). The qadi will pass judgment on the people of the diwan (the new ‘aqila). If judgment had already passed on his desert (village) ‘aqila, it does not transfer.
The diwan receives ‘ata, making payment easier.
4
Summarize the rule for post-judgment changes
In both scenarios, once the qadi has passed judgment, the liability is fixed. Any subsequent change in residence or status does not shift it. The rule prevents naql (transfer) that would nullify the first judgment.

A resident of Kufa who is not from the people of 'ata killed someone, and before the qadi's judgment he made Basra his city, so the qadi's judgment will be on the people of Basra? And upon this, if the killer's residence was in Kufa and he has no 'ata, and judgment was not passed against him until he settled in Basra, judgment of diyah is passed on the people of Basra. And if judgment was passed on the people of Kufa, it does not transfer from them. Translation: And similarly, if the killer's residence is in Kufa and he has no 'ata, judgment of diyah was not passed on him until he made Basra his home, then judgment of diyah will be on the people of Basra, and if judgment of diyah had already been passed on the people of Kufa, then the obligation will not transfer from the people of Kufa. Commentary: In light of the mentioned principle and proof, a second case is presented: a person who is not from ahl al-'ata and is a resident of Kufa killed someone, and the qadi had not yet passed judgment of obligation of diyah when he made Basra his home. Now when the qadi passes judgment of obligation of diyah, he will pass it on the people of Basra. And if the qadi had already passed judgment of obligation of diyah on the people of Kufa before his transfer, then this obligation will not change due to his transfer, otherwise transfer (naql) would occur which is not permissible. A villager commits unintentional homicide and before the qadi's judgment his name enters the army, on whom will the qadi pass judgment of diyah? And similarly, the bedouin, if he is enrolled in the diwan after the killing before judgment, judgment of diyah is passed on the people of the diwan. And after judgment, on his 'aqila in the desert, it does not transfer from them. Translation: And similarly, the bedouin, when he is enrolled with the diwan after the killing before judgment, then judgment of diyah is passed on the people of the diwan. And after passing judgment on his village 'aqila, the obligation does not transfer from them (the village 'aqila). Commentary: In light of this, the second case is presented: a person from a village who killed someone by mistake, and judgment of diyah had not yet been passed on his 'aqila when his name came in the army. Now the qadi passes judgment of diyah, so the qadi will pass judgment of obligation of diyah on the people of the diwan. And if the qadi had already passed judgment of obligation of diyah on his village 'aqila before he was from the people of the diwan, then it will not be on the people of the diwan, and naql will not be necessary.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents two applications of the same principle regarding the transfer of 'aqila liability. The first case: a Kufan resident without 'ata (stipend) kills someone and moves to Basra before the qadi's judgment. The ruling is that judgment goes on Basra's 'aqila because the obligation follows the current residence when not yet fixed by a judgment. If judgment had already been made on Kufa's 'aqila, it does not transfer to Basra. The second case: a villager (bedouin) kills someone and before judgment joins the diwan (military registration). Judgment goes on the diwan's people (military 'ata recipients). But if judgment was already made on his village 'aqila, it does not transfer to the diwan. The underlying principle is that the obligation of diyah on 'aqila is fixed at the time of the qadi's judgment. Any change in the killer's affiliation before judgment changes the liable group; after judgment, the liability is locked in. This prevents naql (transfer of obligation) which would nullify the first judgment. The reasoning is based on istihsan: following the group that is easiest for payment (ahl al-diwan receive 'ata, making payment easier) when judgment hasn't been passed. The commentary clarifies that the same principle applies to both scenarios: before judgment, liability transfers; after judgment, it does not.

Key Terms
دیوان (diwan): The military register; enrollment in the diwan makes one eligible for 'ata (stipend) and may affect 'aqila liability.
عطاء ('ata): State stipend paid to soldiers or registered individuals; it is considered property from which diyah can be taken.
عاقلة ('aqila): The group of male relatives (or military cohort) who collectively pay diyah for unintentional homicide.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The 'aqila of Kufa
B) The 'aqila of Basra
C) Both Kufa and Basra equally
D) The killer's own property only

Key Takeaway
The liability of diyah on the ‘aqila is fixed at the moment of the qadi’s judgment; changes before judgment shift liability, after judgment it remains locked.

Diyat — Blood Money

Paying Diyah from 'Ata After Property Judgment
Ruling on collecting diyah from 'ata after property judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on paying diyah from 'ata after a judgment in property and identify the exception when the species of payment differs.

Core Idea
Diyah is paid from the easiest property; 'ata is property, so it can be used, but the genus of payment cannot be changed.

After a qadi has ruled that diyah must be paid from the 'aqila's property over three years, what happens if the imam later enrolls them in the state stipend ('ata) system? Does the judgment change? This passage clarifies that the payment source switches to 'ata for ease, but only if the species (jins) remains the same.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A person kills by mistake, the qadi orders diyah from the 'aqila's property over three years, then the imam enrolls the family in the 'ata (stipend) system.
The judgment is already issued; the enrollment happens after.
2
State the general rule
The diyah is now taken from the 'ata payments instead of the original property, because 'ata is also property and paying from it is easier.
This does not nullify the first judgment; it only changes the payment source.
3
Identify the reason (illah)
Diyah is taken from the easiest property available (aysar al-amwal). Payment from 'ata is easier when the family receives it.
This is based on the principle of yusr (ease) in paying blood money.
4
Recognize the exception
If the judgment was in a specific species (e.g., camels) and the 'ata is in a different species (dirhams), the judgment cannot be converted to dirhams; the camels must still be paid from the 'ata funds.
Changing the genus (jins) would nullify the original judgment.
5
Apply the rule to a new case
If judgment was in camels and 'ata is in dirhams, the 'aqila must purchase camels using the 'ata money and pay them. The court cannot order dirhams instead.
The species is a fixed element of the judgment.

A person from a village family killed someone by mistake, and the qadi passed judgment of diyah on the 'aqila, to be paid in installments over three years. Then the imam enrolled the entire family among the people of 'ata, so the diyah will now be from those 'ata payments. And this is contrary to the case where a group from the desert people had diyah judged against them in their property over three years, then the imam made them among the 'ata, so the diyah becomes in their 'ata payments, even though the first time judgment was passed in their property, because there is no nullification of the first judgment in it, because judgment was passed in their property, and their 'ata payments are their property, except that diyah is paid from the easiest property for payment, and payment from 'ata is easier when they have become from the people of 'ata. Except if the 'ata property is not of the same species as what was judged against them, such as if judgment was in camels and the 'ata is in dirhams, then in that case it never converts to dirhams, because it would nullify the first judgment, but it is paid from the 'ata property because it is easier. But diyah is paid from that property from which payment is easy, and payment from 'ata is easier when they have become from the people of 'ata. Except when the 'ata property is not of the same species as what was judged against them, such that judgment was in camels and 'ata in dirhams, then in that case diyah never transfers to dirhams, because it would nullify the first judgment. It (the camels) will be paid from the 'ata property because it is easier. Commentary: There is a village family. One of them committed unintentional homicide, so diyah was made obligatory on his 'aqila (the family) in their property, to be paid in installments over three years. But after this judgment, the imam enrolled the entire family among ahl al-'ata and issued a judgment that they receive annual 'ata. So the qadi's judgment of diyah that was in their property now becomes obligatory in their 'ata, and one-third annually will be collected from it. Apparently it seems this breaks the first judgment, but it is not so, because the qadi passed judgment of diyah in their property, and the 'ata is also their property. And it has already passed that diyah is made obligatory on that property from which payment is easiest, and clearly paying diyah from 'ata is easier than paying from the original property. And since they have now become from ahl al-'ata, this principle is observed. Yes, if the qadi had judged diyah in camels, that one hundred camels be paid, and they receive dirhams in 'ata, then it cannot be that the qadi changes his judgment and instead of one hundred camels judges ten thousand dirhams, because that would nullify the first judgment, and
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where diyah has been judged against a group's 'aqila in their property, payable over three years. After the judgment, the imam enrolls them in the 'ata (stipend) system. The ruling: the diyah is then taken from their annual 'ata payments, because 'ata is also their property, and paying from 'ata is easier than taking from their original property. This does not nullify the first judgment; it only changes the source of payment to a more convenient one, based on the principle that diyah is taken from the easiest available property. An exception is made: if the judgment specified payment in a particular species (e.g., camels) and the 'ata is in a different species (dirhams), the judgment cannot be changed to dirhams because that would nullify the first judgment (changing the genus). Instead, the camels are still required, but they are purchased using the 'ata funds and paid. The commentary explains the reasoning: the 'ata is considered property of the recipients, and the principle of ease (yusr) permits switching to a quicker payment method as long as the species does not change. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) to facilitate the payment of diyah without violating the integrity of the original judgment.

Key Terms
عطاء ('ata): State stipend paid to those registered in the diwan; considered property of the recipient for legal purposes.
جنس (jins): Species or genus of property; changing the species in a judgment (e.g., camels to dirhams) nullifies the original judgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The diyah is automatically converted to dirhams.
B) The diyah is paid from the 'ata but still as camels (purchased with dirhams).
C) The judgment is nullified and a new one is issued.
D) The 'aqila is exempted from paying diyah.

Key Takeaway
Diyah is collected from the easiest property available, but the species of payment fixed in the judgment cannot be changed.

Diyat — Blood Money

Change of Aqilah's Payment from Property to Stipend
Ruling on diyah shifting from property to stipend after imam's change

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyah payment shifting from property to stipends and identify the condition that prevents the shift.

Core Idea
Diyah shifts from property to stipends when the aqilah becomes stipend recipients, unless the payment type differs from the stipend currency.

This lesson addresses a practical scenario in the law of diyat (blood money). When a group (āqila) is ordered to pay diyah from their properties over three years, and later the imam enrolls them in the state stipend system (ʿaṭāʾ), does the obligation shift to the stipends? The Hanafi view is that it does shift, because the stipends are also their property and paying from stipends is easier. However, if the original judgment specified a particular type (e.g., camels) and the stipends are in a different type (e.g., dirhams), the shift does not occur; instead, the āqila must purchase the specified type from their stipends. This ruling preserves the original judgment while allowing ease in collection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial judgment
The judge initially orders the āqila to pay diyah from their general properties (amwāl) over three years. This establishes a specific obligation in type (if specified) or at least in value.
The judgment is valid and binding.
2
Consider the change in status of the āqila
After the judgment, the imam enrolls the āqila in the state stipend (ʿaṭāʾ), so they now receive regular payments. The question is whether the diyah obligation can be satisfied from these stipends.
The stipends are a form of property (māl).
3
Apply the principle of ease (aysar)
The Hanafi rule is that diyah is taken from the easiest asset available. Since stipends are easier to collect (instalments from state) than seizing personal property, the obligation shifts to stipends.
This does not nullify the original judgment; it only changes the source of payment.
4
Apply the exception: when payment type differs
If the original judgment specified a particular type (e.g., camels) and the stipends are in a different type (e.g., dirhams), the type cannot be changed. The āqila must use their stipends to purchase the specified type and then pay.
Changing the type would nullify the original judgment.

وهذا بخلاف ما اذا كان قوم من اهل البادية قضى بالدية عليهم فى اموالهم في ثلث سنين ثم جعلهم الامام في العطاء حيث تصير الدية فى عطياتهم وان كان قضى بها اول مرة فى اموالهم لانه ليس فيه نقض القضاء الأول لانه قضى بها فى اموالهم وعطياتهم اموالهم غيران الدية تقضى من ايسر الاموال اداء والاداء من العطاء ايسر اذا صار وامن اهل العطاء الا اذا لم يكن مال العطاء من جنس ما قضى به عليه بان كان القضاء بالابل والعطاء دراهم فحينئذ لا تتحول الى الدراهم ابدا لما فيه من ابطال القضاء الأول لكن يقضى ذلك من مال العطاء لانه ايسر ترجمہ . اور یہ اس صورت کے خلاف ہے جب کہ کوئی قوم اہل دیہات میں سے ہو جن کے اوپر ان کے اموال میں تین سالوں کے اندردیت کا فیصلہ کر دیا گیا پھر امام نے ان کو اہل عطاء میں سے کر دیا تو دیت ان کے عطیات میں واجب ہوگی اگر چہ پہلی مرتبہ دیت کا فیصلہ ان کے اموال میں کر دیا گیا تھا اس لئے کہ اس کے اندر پہلے فیصلہ کو توڑنا نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ دیت کا فیصلہ ان کے اموال میں کیا گیا تھا اور ان کے عطیات ان کے اموال ہیں مگر دیت ادا کیجاتی ہے ان اموال میں سے جن سے ادائیگی سہل ہو اور عطاء میں سے ادا ئیگی سہل ہے جب کہ وہ اہل عطاء میں سے ہو گئے ہیں مگر جب کہ مال عطاء اس حق کی جنس سے نہ ہو جس کا اہل عطا ء پر فیصلہ کیا گیا ہے اس طریقہ پر کہ دیت کا فیصلہ اونٹوں سے ہو اور عطاء درہم ہوں پس اس وقت میں کبھی بھی دیت دراہم کی جانب مقتول نہ ہوگی اس لئے کہ اس میں پہلے فیصلہ کو باطل کرنا ہے اسکو (اونٹوں کو ) عطاء کے مال سے ادا کیا جائے گا اس لئے کہ یہ آسان ہے۔ تشریح ایک گاؤں کا خاندان ہے ان میں سے کسی نے خطا قتل کر دیا تو اس کے عاقلہ یعنی خاندان کے اوپر دبیت کو واجب کر دیا گیا یعنی ان کے اموال میں جو قسط وار تین سالوں میں ادا کر دی جائے گی۔ مگر اس فیصلہ کے بعد ایسا ہوا کہ امام نے اس پورے خاندان کو اہل عطاء میں سے قرار دیدیا اور ان کو سالانہ عطیات ملنے کا فیصلہ صادر فرمادیا تو قاضی نے وجوب دبیت کا جو فیصلہ ان کے اموال میں کیا تھا تو اب دیت ان عطا یا میں واجب ہوگی اور انہی میں سے سالانہ ثلث دیت کو وصول کیا جائے گا اور بظاہر اگر چہ یہاں ایسا معلوم ہو رہا ہے کہ یہ حکم اول اور فیصلہ اول کو توڑنا ہے مگر ایسا نہیں ہے کیونکہ قاضی نے وجوب دیت کا فیصلہ ان کے اموال میں کیا تھا اور عطیات بھی انہیں کے اموال ہیں اور یہ پہلے گزر ہی چکا ہے کہ دیت اس مال میں واجب کی جاتی ہے جس سے دیت کی ادائیگی سب سے آسان ہو اور ظاہر ہے کہ عطا یا میں سے دیت ادا کرنا اصل اموال کے اندر سے ادا کرنے سے بہت آسان ہے اور چونکہ اب یہ اہل عطاء میں سے ہو گئے ہیں تو اصول کا لحاظ رکھا جائے گا۔ ہاں اگر قاضی نے دیت کا فیصلہ ابلوں سے کیا ہو کہ سو اونٹ ادا کئے جائیں اور ان کو عطایا میں دراہم ملتے ہیں تو اب یہ نہیں ہو سکتا کہ قاضی اپنا فیصلہ بدل دے اور بجائے سو اونٹ کے دس ہزار ہم کا فیصلہ کر دے کیونکہ اس میں قضاء اول کا ابطال لازم آتا ہے اور یہ باطل ہے۔ ہاں یہ صورت اختیار کی جائے کہ یہ لوگ عطایا میں سے دیت ادا کریں جس کی صورت یہ ہوگی ۔ کہ وہ ان درا ہم سے اونٹ خرید کر دیت ادا کر دیں۔
And this is contrary to when a group from the desert had the diyah ordered upon them in their properties over three years, then the imam included them among the stipend recipients, so the diyah becomes in their stipends, even if it was ordered the first time upon their properties, because there is no annulment of the first judgment, because it was ordered upon their properties, and their stipends are their properties, except that diyah is taken from the easiest assets to pay, and payment from stipend is easier when they have become stipend recipients. Unless the stipend asset is not of the same type as what was judged, such that the judgment was in camels and the stipend is in dirhams; then it never changes to dirhams because it would nullify the first judgment, but it is paid from the stipend property because it is easier.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage addresses a practical situation where the aqilah (tribe responsible for paying diyah) initially had the diyah imposed on their general properties in three installments. After the judgment, the imam enrolls them in the 'ata (state stipends). The question is whether the diyah must still be paid from their personal properties or can be taken from their stipends. The ruling: the diyah shifts to the stipends because stipends are also their property, and payment from stipends is easier. This does not violate the original judgment because the debt (diyah) remains in their wealth, just collected from a more convenient source. However, if the original judgment specified payment in camels (e.g., 100 camels) and the stipends are in dirhams, then the type cannot be changed, because that would effectively alter the judgment's subject matter. Instead, the recipients must purchase camels from their dirham stipends to fulfill the obligation. This preserves the judgment while allowing ease. The reasoning is based on the principle that diyah is taken from the easiest asset available.

Key Terms
عاقلہ (āqila): The tribe or group responsible for paying diyah (blood money) for accidental killing by one of their members.
عطاء (ʻaṭāʼ): State stipend or allowance given annually to soldiers or registered recipients.
دیت (diya): Blood money; financial compensation for homicide or bodily harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a group is ordered to pay diyah in camels and later receives stipends in dirhams, what must they do?
A) Pay diyah in dirhams instead of camels
B) Purchase camels from dirhams to pay diyah
C) The imam can change the judgment to dirhams
D) The aqilah is no longer responsible

Key Takeaway
The diyah shifts to the easiest available asset, but the type of payment specified in the original judgment must be preserved.

Aqilah of a Freed Slave is the Master's Tribe
Ruling on aqilah for a freed slave with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the aqilah of a freed slave and explain the proof from the hadith.

Core Idea
A freed slave's aqilah is his master's tribe—he is legally one of them for diyah purposes.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in diyat: who bears the diyah for accidental killing committed by a freed slave. The Hanafi school holds that the aqilah is the master's tribe, based on the principle of support (nusra) and a hadith. Understanding this ruling clarifies the legal bond (wala) formed through manumission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The aqilah (responsible group for diyah) of a freed slave. The ruling: it is the tribe of his master.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? The hadith: 'Mawla al-qawm minhum' (the freed slave of a people is from among them).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? It equates the freed slave to his master's people, making them one entity for responsibility.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Does any school disagree? Some schools assign aqilah differently (e.g., Shafi'i: biological family if exist).
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Which position is relied upon? The Hanafi position is mufta bihi, supported by the hadith and the reality that the freed slave has no blood asabah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وعاقلة المعتق قبيلة مولاه لان النصرة بهم ويؤيد ذلك قوله عليه السلام مولى القوم من ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور معشق کا عاقلہ اس کے آقا کا قبیلہ ہے اسلئے کہ نصرت انہی کے ذریعہ سے ہے اور فرمان نبی صلى الله عليه وسلم کا مؤید ہے کہ قوم کا آزاد کردہ غلام اسی قوم میں سے شمار ہوگا۔ تشریح معتق کا عاقلہ معتق اور معتق کا خاندان ہے اس لئے کہ یہ انہی سے مدد طلب کرے گا اور حدیث مذکور سے اس کی تائید ہوگئی۔
He said: And the aqilah of a freed slave is the tribe of his master, because support comes from them. And this is supported by his statement (peace be upon him): 'The freed slave of a people is from among them.'
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This is a ruling stating that the aqilah (responsible group for diyah) of a freed slave (mu'taq) is the tribe of his master (mawla). The reason given is that the freed slave looks to his master's tribe for support (nusra), as he has lost his own family ties. This is further supported by the hadith: 'The freed slave of a people is from among them,' indicating legal inclusion. The commentator explains that the aqilah comprises the master himself and his extended family. Thus, if the freed slave commits an accidental killing, diyah is paid by the master's tribe, not by the freed slave's original family.

Key Terms
معتق (muʻtiq): The master who frees a slave, or the freed slave? Here it refers to the freed slave (contextually mu'taq).
مولى (mawlā): Master; in this context the manumitter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, the aqilah of a freed slave is:
A) His master's tribe
B) His biological family
C) The state
D) Himself

Key Takeaway
The freed slave's aqilah is his master's tribe due to the hadith and the principle of nusra.

Aqilah of Mawla al-Muwalat: The Patron's Tribe is Responsible
Ruling on aqilah for mawla al-muwalat with Shafi'i difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who serves as aqilah for a client by mutual patronage (mawla al-muwalat) and explain the Shafi'i disagreement and its practical consequence.

Core Idea
A client by mutual patronage is like a freed slave — his patron's tribe pays his diyah.

This passage from al-Hidayah clarifies who bears the diyah (blood money) for a person who has entered into a contract of mutual patronage (muwalat). The Hanafi school extends the same aqilah system used for emancipated slaves to this type of client, while Imam al-Shafi'i rejects the validity of the contract altogether. Understanding this difference is crucial for knowing who is financially responsible when a mawla al-muwalat commits an accidental killing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Parties
The passage discusses two persons: the mawla al-muwalat (client) and his mawla (patron) along with the patron's tribe. Determine who pays diyah.
2
State the Hanafi Ruling
The patron and his tribe are the aqilah. Understand the basis: analogy to wala' al-itiq (patronage by emancipation).
The 'illa (effective cause) is mutual support (tanasur).
3
Analyze the Analogy (Qiyas)
The passage draws an analogy between muwalat and emancipation. Identify the shared 'illa of mutual support.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
4
Contrast with Shafi'i View
Imam al-Shafi'i denies the validity of the muwalat contract. Examine his proof and the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).
Shafi'i: client pays himself if he can.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi. Understand why the school gives preference to this view.
Supported by practice and analogy; not explicitly prohibited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومولى الموالاة يعقل عنه مولاه وقبيلته لانه ولاء يتنا صربه فاشبه ولا العتاقة وفيه خلاف الشافعي وقد مر في الولاء ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور مولی الموالات اس کی جانب سے اس کا مولی ولاء دبیت دے گا اور اس کا قبیلہ اس لئے کہ یہ ایسا ولاء ہے جس کی وجہ سے باہمی تناصر ہوتا ہے تو یہ ولاء عتاقہ کے مشابہ ہو گیا اور اس میں شافعی کا اختلاف ہے اور یہ ولاء میں گذر چکا ہے۔ تشریح عقد موالات ہمارے نزدیک درست ہے اور امام شافعی کے نزدیک باطل ہے لہذا ہمارے نزدیک اگر دو شخصوں میں عقد موالات قائم ہوا اور اس سے کوئی خطا سرزد ہوئی تو اس کا مولی موالات اور مولی کا خاندان اس کی دیت ادا کرے گا کیونکہ جس طرح ولاء عناقه با ہمی تناصر کا ذریعہ ہے اسی طرح ولاء موالات بھی باہمی تناصر کا ذریعہ ہے ہدایہ جلد ثالث میں کتاب الولاء گذر چکی ہے۔
He said: And the client by mutual agreement (mawla al-muwalat) - his mawla and his tribe pay diyah on his behalf, because it is a patronage that entails mutual support, so it resembles the patronage of emancipation. There is a difference with al-Shafi'i in this, and it has passed in the Book of Patronage.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage discusses the aqilah for a person who has entered into a contract of mutual patronage (mawla al-muwalat) - an agreement where two unrelated persons pledge mutual support and inheritance rights. The Hanafi ruling: the aqilah is the patron (mawla) and his tribe. The reasoning is qiyas (analogy) to the patronage of emancipation (wala' al-itiq), because both involve mutual support. The opposition from al-Shafi'i is mentioned: Imam al-Shafi'i considers the contract of muwalat invalid, thus no such aqilah responsibility. The Hanafi school upholds its validity based on the principle of istihsan and support from the sunnah. The commentator clarifies that this topic was already covered in the Book of Wala' in volume three of al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
مولى الموالاة (mawlā al-muwālāt): A client by mutual patronage contract; a person who enters an agreement with another for mutual support and inheritance.
ولاء (walāʼ): Patronage; a legal bond of loyalty and support, either by emancipation or agreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does Imam al-Shafi'i differ regarding mawla al-muwalat?
A) He considers it valid but with different conditions
B) He considers the contract invalid
C) He agrees completely with Hanafi
D) He requires a different amount of diyah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the patron and his tribe are the aqilah for a client by mutual patronage, by analogy to emancipation, while the Shafi'i school rejects the contract.

Minimum Amount for Aqilah Liability
Ruling with proof on aqilah's minimum liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum compensation amount for which the aqilah is liable, cite the hadith proof, and explain the rationale of relieving hardship.

Core Idea
The aqilah only pays for half a tenth of diyah or more; lesser amounts are borne by the individual.

The aqilah system ensures that accidental harm does not crush an individual with debt. But this relief has a threshold: very small compensations remain the responsibility of the wrongdoer. This lesson establishes that threshold at half a tenth of full diyah, based on a clear hadith and the principle of avoiding hardship (iḥjāf).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the aqilah's payment of diyah or arsh for accidental injury. The ruling is that the aqilah is liable only when the amount is at least half a tenth (nisf ‘ushr) of the full diyah for a life.
Full diyah = 100 camels or equivalent; half a tenth = 5 camels.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The primary proof is the hadith of Ibn Abbas (marfu‘) listing six categories the aqilah does not pay, including 'less than arsh al-muḍiḥa'. The arsh of muḍiḥa (a wound exposing bone) is set at half a tenth, thereby establishing the minimum.
The hadith also excludes intentional killing, slave, settlement, and confession—these are paid by the individual.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah (point of inference)
The phrase 'nor less than arsh al-muḍiḥa' directly excludes amounts below half a tenth. Since aqilah is not responsible for those, the threshold is exactly the arsh of muḍiḥa. The companion's statement indicates this is the dividing line.
The hadith is zannī al-thubūt, but accepted in practice.
4
Understand the rationale (ḥikmah)
The aqilah was instituted to prevent extreme hardship (iḥjāf) on the individual. Small amounts do not cause such hardship, so they remain on the individual. The specific numerical threshold is known through divine revelation (sam‘).
This rational argument supports the textual proof.
5
Apply the ruling
For any injury where the arsh is less than half a tenth of full diyah, the individual (jānī) pays. For half a tenth or more, the aqilah pays. In practice, this means minor wounds are the individual's responsibility.
If multiple injuries combine to reach the threshold, the aqilah is liable for the total once it meets the minimum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولا تعقل العاقلة اقل من نصف عشر الدية وتتحمل نصف العشر فصاعد او الاصل فيه حديث ابن عباس رضى الله عنه موقوفا عليه مرفوعا الى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لا تعقل العواقل عمدا ولا عبدا ولا صلحا ولا اعترفا ولا ما دون ارش الموضحة وارش الموضحة نصف عشر بدل النفس ولان التحمل للتحرز عن الاحجاف ولا اف فى القليل وانما هو فى الكثير والتقدير الفاصل عرف بالسمع احجاف ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور عاقلہ دیت کے نصف عشر سے کم کی دیت نہیں دیں گے اور عاقلہ نصف عشر یازیادہ کا عمل کریں گے اور اصل اس میں ابن عباس رضی اللہ عنہ کی حدیث ہے جو ان موقوف ہے اور رسول الشہور تک مرفوع ہے کہ عواقل عمر کی دیت نہیں دیتے اور نہ نام کی اور نہ صلح کی اور نہ اعتراف کی اور نہ موضحہ کے ارش سے کم کی اور موضحہ کا ارش نفس کے بدل کا نصف عشر ہے اور اس لئے کہ حمل عاقلہ احجاف کے لئے ہے اور نہیں ہے وہ قلیل میں بلکہ وہ کثیر میں ہے اور تقدیر کا فاصل عرف سے سمع سے معلوم ہوا۔
He said: And the aqilah does not pay for less than half a tenth of the diyah, and they bear half a tenth and above. The basis for this is the hadith of Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, as a report from him and raised to the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him: 'The aqilah do not pay for intentional killing, nor for a slave, nor for a settlement, nor for a confession, nor for less than the arsh of a head wound (mudihah). And the arsh of a head wound is half a tenth of the blood money for a life.' And because bearing (the liability) is to avoid hardship (ihjaf), and it is not for small amounts but for large ones, and the dividing estimation is known by sound transmission.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage establishes the minimum threshold for aqilah liability. The aqilah (tribe) is responsible for paying diyah only when the amount is at least half a tenth (nisf 'ushr) of the full diyah for a life. Full diyah for a free man is 100 camels or equivalent; half a tenth = 5 camels or equivalent. For lesser amounts, the individual himself pays. The proof is a hadith from Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) stating the aqilah does not pay for intentional killing (the individual pays), nor for a slave (the master pays), nor for settlement (sulh) nor confession (i'tiraf), nor for wounds less than a mudihah. A mudihah is a wound that exposes the bone; its arsh (compensation) is half a tenth of full diyah. The rationale is that aqilah's responsibility is to prevent hardship (ihjaf), which only occurs with substantial amounts, not trivial ones. The precise threshold is established through transmitted texts.

Key Terms
ارش الموضحة (arsh al-mūḍiḥa): The compensation for a head wound that exposes the bone; its amount is half a tenth of full diyah.
احجاف (iḥjāf): Hardship; the aqilah system was established to prevent individuals from facing excessive financial burden.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minimum amount that the aqilah is required to pay?
A) Full diyah
B) Half a tenth of full diyah
C) One-tenth of full diyah
D) One-twentieth of full diyah

Key Takeaway
The aqilah's liability begins at half a tenth of full diyah, proven by hadith and the principle of avoiding hardship.

Ruling on Paying Diyah from Stipends by Purchasing Camels
Ruling statement on a permissible method for diyah payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the permissible method for the 'Aqila to pay diyah when they have dirhams from stipends.

Core Idea
Diyah is paid in camels, but stipends can be used to buy camels.

In the Hanafi school, diyah (blood money) is traditionally paid in camels. However, the 'Aqila (the responsible group) may be given dirhams as stipends from the state. This lesson explains a practical solution allowing them to use those dirhams to purchase camels and fulfill their obligation, based on the principle of ease.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the permissible act? The 'Aqila may use their stipends (dirhams) to buy camels and pay those camels as diyah. The ruling is mubah (permissible).
2
Understand the condition
The 'Aqila must have received stipends in the form of dirhams. They must specifically use those dirhams to purchase camels, not pay directly in dirhams or other goods.
3
Note the purpose
The purpose is to facilitate payment of diyah while adhering to the principle that diyah is paid in camels. This avoids hardship if the 'Aqila does not have camels readily.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ہاں یہ صورت اختیار کی جائے کہ یہ لوگ عطایا میں سے دیت ادا کریں جس کی صورت یہ ہوگی ۔ کہ وہ ان درا ہم سے اونٹ خرید کر دیت ادا کر دیں۔
Yes, this form can be adopted that these people pay the diyah from the stipends, the form of which is that they buy camels from these dirhams and pay the diyah.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the commentary of Al-Hidayah. It provides a practical solution for the 'Aqila (the tribe or group responsible for paying blood money) when they have dirhams instead of camels. Since diyah is traditionally paid in camels, they are allowed to use the dirhams from their 'ataya (stipends) to purchase camels and then pay the diyah. This ruling is based on the principle of ease and removing hardship.

Key Terms
عطایا (‘aṭāyā): Stipends or grants given by the state to the 'Aqila members
دیت (diyah): Blood money paid for unintentional killing or injury
عاقلہ (‘āqilah): The group of male relatives or tribe responsible for paying diyah on behalf of the offender

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the permissible method described for 'Aqila to pay diyah when they have dirhams?
A) Pay the diyah in dirhams directly.
B) Use the dirhams to buy camels and pay those camels.
C) Give the dirhams to the victim's family as compensation.
D) The 'Aqila is not required to pay if they have dirhams.

Key Takeaway
The 'Aqila may use state stipends to purchase camels and pay diyah, ensuring the payment is in the prescribed form.

'Aqila of a Client by Contract: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Ruling with inter-madhhab dispute on 'Aqila for mawla al-muwalat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the 'Aqila of a client by contract and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position, using the analogy to emancipation.

Core Idea
The contract of muwalat makes the patron's tribe the 'Aqila by analogy to emancipation.

This lesson examines a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the contract of clientage (muwalat). The Hanafi school, following Quduri, holds that such a contract is valid and that the patron and his tribe are responsible for the diyah of the client. The Shafi'i school disagrees, rejecting the contract's validity. The Hanafi proof rests on an analogy to the wala of emancipation, which is universally accepted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The core dispute is whether a contract of muwalat (clientage by agreement) is valid and creates 'Aqila responsibility for diyah.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
The Hanafi school (Quduri) says the contract is valid; the patron and his tribe pay diyah for the client. The proof is qiyas (analogy) to wala of emancipation: both involve mutual assistance (tanasur).
The text mentions the proof has 'already passed' in the book of Wala.
3
Identify the opposite school's view
Imam al-Shafi'i considers the contract of muwalat invalid. Therefore, no 'Aqila obligation arises; the client bears his own diyah.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In the Hanafi school, if a client by contract accidentally kills someone, his mawla and tribe pay diyah. In the Shafi'i school, the client pays alone.
This difference affects how liability is assigned.
5
Understand the underlying analogy
The Hanafi proof depends on the shared illa (reason) of mutual assistance. Both forms of wala create a bond of support, so the same ruling on 'Aqila applies.
This is a clear example of qiyas jaliy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مولی الموالات کی عاقلہ مولیٰ کا خاندان ہے
قال ومولى الموالاة يعقل عنه مولاه وقبيلته لانه ولاء يتنا صربه فاشبه ولا العتاقة وفيه خلاف الشافعي وقد مر في الولاء
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور مولی الموالات اس کی جانب سے اس کا مولی ولاء دبیت دے گا اور اس کا قبیلہ اس لئے کہ یہ ایسا ولاء ہے جس کی وجہ سے باہمی تناصر ہوتا ہے تو یہ ولاء عتاقہ کے مشابہ ہو گیا اور اس میں شافعی کا اختلاف ہے اور یہ ولاء میں گذر چکا ہے۔ تشریح عقد موالات ہمارے نزدیک درست ہے اور امام شافعی کے نزدیک باطل ہے لہذا ہمارے نزدیک اگر دو شخصوں میں عقد موالات قائم ہوا اور اس سے کوئی خطا سرزد ہوئی تو اس کا مولی موالات اور مولی کا خاندان اس کی دیت ادا کرے گا کیونکہ جس طرح ولاء عناقه با ہمی تناصر کا ذریعہ ہے اسی طرح ولاء موالات بھی باہمی تناصر کا ذریعہ ہے ہدایہ جلد ثالث میں کتاب الولاء گذر چکی ہے۔
The 'Aqila of the client by contract is the family of his mawla. He said: And the client by contract, his mawla and his tribe pay the diyah for him because it is a wala that entails mutual assistance, so it resembles the wala of emancipation. And in this there is disagreement of al-Shafi'i, and it has passed in the book of Wala. [Quduri's text] Quduri said: And the mawla al-muwalat, his mawla and his tribe will pay the diyah on his behalf because this wala entails mutual assistance, so it resembles wala of emancipation. And Shafi'i disagrees. And it has passed in the book of Wala. Explanation: Contract of Muwalat is valid in our school, and invalid according to Imam Shafi'i. Therefore, in our school, if two persons establish a contract of muwalat and an error occurs, his mawla al-muwalat and his mawla's family will pay his diyah because just as wala of emancipation is a means of mutual assistance, similarly wala of muwalat is also a means of mutual assistance. This has passed in volume three of Hidayah in the book of Wala.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the validity and effect of a contract of muwalat (clientage by contract). The Hanafi position (as stated by Al-Quduri) is that such a contract is valid, and the 'Aqila for the client (mawla al-muwalat) is his mawla (the patron) and the mawla's tribe. This is because the wala (clientage) established by contract involves mutual assistance (tanasur), similar to the wala of emancipation ('itaq). Since the wala of emancipation makes the master's tribe responsible for the diyah, the same applies here. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the contract of muwalat is invalid, and therefore no such responsibility arises. The commentary confirms that in the Hanafi school, if a contract of muwalat is made and the client commits an error requiring diyah, the mawla and his tribe pay it.

Key Terms
موالات (muwālāt): A contract of clientage between two persons, establishing mutual inheritance and responsibility
ولاء (walā'): Clientage relationship, either by emancipation or by contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the Hanafi ruling on the 'Aqila of a client by contract?
A) The contract is invalid, so the client pays his own diyah.
B) The contract is valid, and the client's own tribe is the 'Aqila.
C) The contract is valid, and the mawla's tribe is the 'Aqila.
D) The contract is valid only for inheritance, not for diyah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates the contract of muwalat and makes the patron's tribe the 'Aqila by analogy to emancipation, contrary to the Shafi'i view.

Minimum Amount for 'Aqila's Liability
Ruling with hadith proof on minimum 'Aqila liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the threshold of 'Aqila liability (half-tenth of full diyah) and identify the hadith that establishes it.

Core Idea
The 'Aqila pays diyah only for half-tenth or more; below that, the killer pays.

In Hanafi fiqh, the 'Aqila (the killer's tribe) is responsible for paying diyah in cases of accidental killing. However, this responsibility is not unlimited; there is a specific minimum amount. This lesson examines the ruling from the hadith of Ibn Abbas, which sets the threshold at half of one-tenth (1/20) of the full diyah. Understanding this threshold is crucial for correctly assigning liability.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify what Al-Quduri states: The 'Aqila does not pay less than half-tenth, and bears half-tenth and above.
The ruling is clear: threshold = 1/20 of full diyah.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the hadith of Ibn Abbas quoted in the text. Note that it is both mauquf and marfu'.
The hadith lists five exemptions.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith explicitly excludes amounts below the 'arsh of mudihah, which is half-tenth. This directly proves the threshold.
The phrase 'wa la ma dun 'arsh al-mudihah' is the key.
4
Understand the rationale
The commentary explains that the 'Aqila's obligation is to prevent hardship (iljaf). Small amounts do not cause hardship, so they are excluded. The distinction is from revelation.
Reason alone cannot determine the threshold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولا تعقل العاقلة اقل من نصف عشر الدية وتتحمل نصف العشر فصاعد او الاصل فيه حديث ابن عباس رضى الله عنه موقوفا عليه مرفوعا الى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لا تعقل العواقل عمدا ولا عبدا ولا صلحا ولا اعترفا ولا ما دون ارش الموضحة وارش الموضحة نصف عشر بدل النفس ولان التحمل للتحرز عن الاحجاف ولا اف فى القليل وانما هو فى الكثير والتقدير الفاصل عرف بالسمع
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور عاقلہ دیت کے نصف عشر سے کم کی دیت نہیں دیں گے اور عاقلہ نصف عشر یازیادہ کا عمل کریں گے اور اصل اس میں ابن عباس رضی اللہ عنہ کی حدیث ہے جو ان موقوف ہے اور رسول الشہور تک مرفوع ہے کہ عواقل عمر کی دیت نہیں دیتے اور نہ نام کی اور نہ صلح کی اور نہ اعتراف کی اور نہ موضحہ کے ارش سے کم کی اور موضحہ کا ارش نفس کے بدل کا نصف عشر ہے اور اس لئے کہ حمل عاقلہ احجاف سے تحرز کے لئے ہے اور قلیل میں احجاف نہیں ہے اور احجاف کثیر میں ہے اور قلیل و کثیر کے درمیان حد فاصل کی نقدیر سمع سے معلوم ہوگئی ہے۔
تشریح عاقلہ پر دیت کا وجوب اس لئے ہوا ہے تا کہ قاتل پریشانیوں میں مبتلا نہ ہو جائے اور پریشانی میں مبتلا ہونا اسی وقت ہوگا جبکہ دیت زیادہ ہواور اگر قلیل ہو تو پریشانی میں مبتلا نہ ہوگا پھر قلیل و کثیر کے درمیان تحدید معقل سے معلوم نہیں ہوسکتی بلکہ یہ بات نص سے معلوم ہوگئی اور نصر نے بتا دیا ہے کہ نصف عشر سے کم یعنی اگر پوری دیت کے بیسویں حصہ سے بھی کم دیت واجب ہو تو اس کو عاقلہ ادا نہیں کرے گا بلکہ اس کو قاتل ادا کرے گا، ہاں اگر بیسواں حصہ یا اس سے زیادہ مقدار دیت کی واجب ہو تو اس کو کثیر شمار کیا جائے گا اور دیت عاقلہ پر واجب ہوگی۔
-1 یہاں ابن عباس کی ایک حدیث ہے جو موقوع اور مرفوعاً دونوں طرح مروی ہے اس میں پانچ چیز میں ارشاد فرمائی گئی ہیں۔ قتل عمد کی ذمہ داری عاقلہ پر نہیں ہے کمائر - اگر غلام نے قتل کیا ہو تو اس کی ذمہ داری آقا کے خاندان پر نہیں ہے بلکہ ولی جنایت کو یہ غلام ہی دید یا جائے گا۔ -۳- اگر قاتل نے عمد اقتل کیا ہو اور قاتل نے مال کی ادائے گی پر مصالحت کرلی ہوتو یہ مال عاقلہ پر واجب نہ ہوگا بلکہ قاتل کے مال میں واجب ہوگا۔ -۴- اگر قاتل نے خود قتل کا اقرار کیا ہے اور عاقلہ اس کی تکذیب کریں تو عاقلہ پر دیت واجب نہ ہوگی۔ -۵- موضحہ کے ارش سے کم جو مالی تاوان واجب ہوتا ہے اس کو عاقلہ نہیں دیں گے بلکہ اس کا وجوب قاتل کے مال میں ہو گا۔ اور ماقبل میں گذر چکا ہے کہ موضحہ میں نصف عشر یعنی بیسواں واجب ہوتا ہے، لہذا معلوم ہوا کہ اس سے کم کے عواقل متحمل نہ ہوں گے۔
He said: The 'Aqila does not pay less than half of one-tenth of the diyah, and they bear half of one-tenth and above. The basis for this is the hadith of Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) as a mauquf and marfu' to the Prophet: 'The 'Aqila do not pay for intentional killing, nor for a slave, nor for a settlement, nor for a confession, nor for less than the 'arsh of al-mudihah.' And the 'arsh of al-mudihah is half of one-tenth of the full blood-money. And because the responsibility of the 'Aqila is to prevent hardship; there is no hardship in small amounts, only in large amounts. The distinguishing measure is known by revelation. Quduri said: The 'Aqila will not pay less than half of one-tenth of the diyah, and the 'Aqila will bear half of one-tenth or more. The basis is the hadith of Ibn Abbas... Explanation: The obligation of diyah on the 'Aqila is to prevent the killer from falling into distress. Distress occurs only when the diyah is large; if it is small, he will not be distressed. The distinction between small and large cannot be determined by reason but by text. The text tells that below half-tenth (i.e., less than one-twentieth of full diyah) the 'Aqila does not pay; the offender pays. If the amount is half-tenth or more, it is considered large and the 'Aqila is obligated. -1: The hadith of Ibn Abbas is narrated both mauquf and marfu', and it mentions five things: (1) The 'Aqila is not responsible for intentional killing. (2) If a slave kills, his responsibility is not on the master's family; the guardian takes the slave. (3) If the killer makes a settlement in exchange for wealth, that wealth is not on the 'Aqila but on the killer's wealth. (4) If the killer confesses to killing and the 'Aqila denies him, the diyah is not on the 'Aqila. (5) The 'Aqila does not pay less than the 'arsh of al-mudihah; it is on the killer's wealth. It has passed before that al-mudihah requires half-tenth, i.e., one-twentieth; thus, less than that the 'Aqila does not bear.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof regarding the minimum amount for which the 'Aqila is liable. The author first cites Al-Quduri's statement: The 'Aqila is not liable for less than half of one-tenth of the full diyah (i.e., one-twentieth). They bear half-tenth and above. The proof is the hadith of Ibn Abbas, which lists five cases where the 'Aqila is not responsible: intentional killing, killing by a slave, settlement, confession, and any amount less than the 'arsh of al-mudihah. The 'arsh of al-mudihah itself is half-tenth of full diyah. Thus, the threshold is half-tenth. The rationale is that the 'Aqila's obligation is to prevent hardship (iljaf) on the killer; small amounts do not cause hardship, so the killer pays. The distinction between small and large is determined by revelation, not reasoning. The commentary elaborates on the five exceptions, clarifying that in cases of 'amd, slave, sulh, i'tiraf, and amounts less than 'arsh mudihah, the 'Aqila is not liable; the payment falls on the killer's own property.

Key Terms
نصف عشر (nisf 'ushr): Half of one-tenth, i.e., one-twentieth (1/20) of full diyah
موضحہ (mūḍiḥah): A wound that lays bare the bone
ارش (arsh): Fixed compensation for a specific injury

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Half of full diyah
B) Half of one-tenth (1/20) or more
C) Less than half of one-tenth (1/20)
D) Any amount

Key Takeaway
The 'Aqila is only liable for diyah amounts of half-tenth (1/20) or more, based on the explicit text of the hadith.
