Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
8 min
Amount Below Half-Tenth is on Offender's Wealth
Inter-madhhab dispute on qiyas vs. istihsan for amounts below half-tenth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on who pays diyah amounts below half-tenth and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position by identifying the evidence (riwaya) and the role of istihsan.

Core Idea
For diyah amounts below half-tenth, Hanafis place liability on the offender, not the 'Aqila, based on revealed texts that override qiyas.

This passage examines a classic dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding liability for small diyah amounts. While qiyas might suggest uniformity, the Hanafis rely on specific narrations that set a threshold at half-tenth. Understanding this distinction clarifies how the school uses istihsan over pure analogy in blood-money cases.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: amounts less than half-tenth are wajib on the offender's own wealth, not on the 'Aqila.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the 'Aqila is liable for all diyah amounts, large and small, based on qiyas.
The Shafi'i view treats all amounts uniformly.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent argues by qiyas: there is no valid distinction between small and large amounts, so the 'Aqila should always be liable.
Qiyas is the default method in the absence of text.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis abandon qiyas (istihsan) due to explicit narrations: the hadith of 'arsh janin (half-tenth) and the hadith of Ibn Abbas. These set a fixed threshold, so amounts below are treated as property damage.
Istihsan is preferred over qiyas when a stronger evidence (riwaya) indicates a distinction.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafi – minor injuries paid by offender; major from 'Aqila. Shafi'i – all from 'Aqila.
This affects how compensation is sought in court.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کتنی مقدار قلیل ہے، جنایت کرنے والے کے مال میں کب فیصلہ کیا جائے گا
قال وما نقص من ذلك يكون فى مال الجانى والقياس فيه التسوية بين القليل والكثير فيجب الكل على العاقلة كما ذهب اليه الشافعى او التسوية فى ان لا يجب على العاقلة شئى الا انا تركناه بما رويناه و بماروى انه علیه السلام اوجب ارش الجنين على العاقلة وهو نصف عشر بدل الرجل على ما مر في الديات فما دونه یسلک به مسلك الاموال لانه يجب بالتحكيم كما يجب ضمان المال بالتقويم فلهذا كان في مال الجاني اخذا بالقياس.
How much is considered small? When is it decided to be on the property of the offender? He said: And what is less than that is in the wealth of the offender. Qiyas in this would be equality between small and large, so the whole would be upon the 'Aqila, as al-Shafi'i held, or equality in that nothing is upon the 'Aqila. But we abandon that because of the riwaya we have narrated, and because of what is narrated that the Prophet obligated the 'arsh of the fetus on the 'Aqila, which is half of one-tenth of the full blood-money of a man, as has passed in the book of Diyat. So what is below that is treated as wealth, because it is obligated by arbitration (tahkim) just as the guarantee of property is obligated by valuation (taqwim). For this reason, it is on the property of the offender, taking qiyas.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute involving qiyas and istihsan. The author states that for amounts less than half-tenth, the obligation falls on the offender's own wealth. Qiyas (analogy) would suggest either complete equality: either the 'Aqila is liable for all amounts (large and small) or not liable for any. Imam al-Shafi'i adopted the first view, making the 'Aqila liable for all diyah amounts. The Hanafi school rejects this and takes istihsan (juristic preference) based on two narrations: the hadith of Ibn Abbas already mentioned, and the hadith where the Prophet made the 'Aqila liable for the 'arsh of a fetus (half-tenth of full diyah). Since the hadith sets the threshold at half-tenth, any amount below that is treated like a property matter (amwal) rather than diyah proper. The Hanafi argument uses a distinction: diyah amounts above the threshold are compensated by 'Uqul (blood wit) which is on the 'Aqila, while amounts below are like property damage (gharam) assessed by arbitration, and thus remain on the offender.

Key Terms
تحکیم (taḥkīm): Arbitration; used here to distinguish fixed diyah from discretionary compensation
تقویم (taqwīm): Valuation; the process of determining property compensation
عاقلة (ʿāqila): The paternal male relatives of the offender who collectively pay diyah for unintentional killings or injuries
أرش (arsh): Fixed compensation for specific bodily injuries, not full diyah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the Hanafi position that small diyah amounts are on the offender's wealth?
A) Qiyas on larger amounts
B) Istihsan based on hadith of 'arsh janin
C) Ijma of the Companions
D) Local custom (urf)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses a revealed threshold (half-tenth) to separate 'Aqila liability from personal liability, applying istihsan over qiyas.

Diyat — Blood Money

Threshold for 'Aqila Liability: Nisf 'Ushr
Determining the minimum amount of diyah that obligates the 'aqila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi threshold for 'aqila liability, explain the textual evidence from the hadith of arsh al-janin, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i qiyas.

Core Idea
The 'aqila is liable only for diyah of half a tenth or more; lesser amounts fall on the killer – a distinction based on revealed text, not reason.

Why does the Hanafi school draw a line at half a tenth? This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains that the purpose of 'aqila liability is to prevent hardship (ihjaf) for the killer. Hardship only arises from large diyah amounts. The exact threshold, however, is not determined by us but by revelation (nass). The key proof is the Prophet's ruling that the arsh of a fetus (half a tenth) is on the 'aqila, which sets the minimum. In this lesson, we will unpack the ruling, its evidence, and the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i counter-argument based on qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its rationale
State the Hanafi position: the 'aqila is liable for diyah of half a tenth or more. Understand that the rationale is to avoid hardship (ihjaf) for the killer: a small amount does not cause hardship, so the killer pays it himself.
The threshold is half a tenth (1/20) of the full diyah.
2
Locate the textual evidence
Find the hadith cited: the Prophet (peace be upon him) made the arsh of a fetus (half a tenth) obligatory on the 'aqila. This is the primary dalil.
The hadith is narrated from Ibn Abbas, both mauquf and marfu'.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the hadith proves the threshold: since the Prophet obligated exactly half a tenth on the 'aqila, amounts less than that are not included, otherwise they would have been mentioned.
The implication (mafhum) is that lesser amounts are on the killer.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i qiyas
Note that Imam al-Shafi'i uses qiyas to argue that all diyah, small or large, should be on the 'aqila, as there is no distinction in the underlying rationale of collective responsibility.
The Hanafi response is to abandon the conflicting qiyas in favor of the explicit nass.
5
Understand the tarjih mechanism
Recognize that the Hanafi preference for nass over qiyas is a standard usul principle. The mufta bihi position is that the 'aqila bears diyah only of half a tenth or more.
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is relied upon in fatwa.

Source Text

پس جو اس سے کم ہو اس کو اموال کے درجہ میں رکھا جائے گا اس لئے کہ یہ (اس سے کم ) حکم کے فیصلہ سے واجب ہو گا جیسے مال کا ضمان تقویم کی وجہ سے واجب ہوتا ہے پس اس وجہ سے وہ ( نصف عشر سے کم ) مجرم کے مال میں ہو گا قیاس پر عمل کرتے ہوئے۔ تشریح نصف عشر سے جو کم ہے وہ عاقلہ پر نہیں بلکہ قاتل کے مال میں واجب ہے۔ کیونکہ قیاس تو یہاں متعارض ہیں ایک قیاس چاہتا ہے کہ قلیل و کثیر کے درمیان فرق کئے بغیر سب دیت عاقلہ پر واجب ہو اور دوسرا قیاس چاہتا ہے کہ عاقلہ پر بالکل واجب نہ ہو بلکہ سب قاتل پر واجب ہو۔
اس لئے ہم نے قیاسوں کو چھوڑ کر نص پر عمل کیا ہے۔
اور نص مذکور سے صاف معلوم ہو گیا کہ نصف عشر سے کم کا ذمہ دار قاتل ہے۔
اور دوسری حدیث یہ بھی ہے کہ نبی کریم صلى الله عليه وسلم نے عاقلہ پر جنین کے ارش کو واجب کیا جو مرد کی دیت کا بلا ہے تو جو مقدار اس سے کم ہو گی اس کو اموال کے درجہ میں رکھا جائے گا کیونکہ جس طرح مال کا ضمان تقویم سے واجب ہوتا ہے اسی طرح نصف عشر سے کم تحکیم ۔ واجب ہوتا ہے لہذا اس کو مجرم کے مال میں واجب کر دیا جائے گا۔
Book of 'Aqila

It is to avoid causing hardship (ihjaf), and hardship does not exist in small amounts but exists in large amounts. The dividing line between small and large has been learned from revelation (sam').
Explanation: The obligation of diyah upon the 'aqila is so that the killer does not fall into difficulties. He would fall into difficulties only when the diyah is large; if it is small, he will not fall into difficulties. Then the definition of small and large cannot be known by reason but by text (nass). The text has informed that less than half a tenth — i.e., if the diyah owed is less than one-twentieth of the full diyah — the 'aqila will not pay it; rather the killer will pay it. Yes, if one-twentieth or more is owed, it is considered large and the 'aqila is obliged.
-1
Here is a hadith from Ibn Abbas, narrated both mauquf and marfu', mentioning five things: (1) The responsibility for intentional killing is not on the 'aqila. (2) If a slave killed, the responsibility is not on the master's family; rather the guardian of the victim is given the slave. (3) If the killer committed intentional killing and then settled for payment of property, that property is not obligatory on the 'aqila but on the killer's own wealth. (4) If the killer confessed to the killing and the 'aqila denies it, the diyah is not obligatory on the 'aqila. (5) For less than the arsh of a mawdihah (wound exposing bone), the financial compensation is not paid by the 'aqila but its obligation is on the killer's wealth. It has passed earlier that in mawdihah, half a tenth (one-twentieth) is obligatory. So it is clear that less than that the 'aqila do not bear.
How much is small? When is it decided in the criminal's wealth?
He (al-Quduri) said: And what is less than that is in the criminal's wealth. The qiyas (analogy) therein would be equality between small and large, so the whole would be obligatory on the 'aqila as al-Shafi'i held, or equality that nothing is obligatory on the 'aqila — but we abandoned that due to the narration we related and due to what is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) made the arsh of the fetus obligatory on the 'aqila, and it is half a tenth of a man's diyah as passed in the book of diyat. So what is less than that is treated like property matters because it is obligatory by tahkim (arbitration) just as guarantee of property is obligatory by taqwim (valuation). Hence it is in the criminal's wealth, acting upon qiyas.
Translation: Al-Quduri said: And what is less than that is in the criminal's wealth. Qiyas would require equality between small and large so the whole diyah would be on the 'aqila as al-Shafi'i held, or equality that nothing is obligatory on the 'aqila. We abandoned that due to the narration we related and due to the hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) made the arsh of the fetus obligatory on the 'aqila, and it is half a tenth of a man's diyah according to the detail passed in the book of diyat.

So what is less than that is placed in the category of property (amwal) because it becomes obligatory by the decree of the judge (tahkim) just as property guarantee is obligatory by valuation (taqwim). Therefore it is in the criminal's wealth, acting upon qiyas. Explanation: What is less than half a tenth is not on the 'aqila but is obligatory in the wealth of the killer. Because here the qiyas are conflicting: one qiyas demands that without distinction between small and large, the whole diyah be on the 'aqila, and another qiyas demands that it not be on the 'aqila at all but on the killer. So we abandoned the qiyas and acted upon the text (nass). And from the mentioned text it became clear that the responsible for less than half a tenth is the killer. And there is also another hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) made the arsh of the fetus obligatory on the 'aqila, which is one-twentieth of a man's diyah. So any amount less than that will be placed in the category of property because just as property guarantee is obligatory by valuation, similarly less than half a tenth becomes obligatory by tahkim. Hence it is made obligatory in the criminal's wealth.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains the threshold for 'aqila liability in unintentional homicide. The ruling: the 'aqila (paternal male relatives) are liable only for diyah amounts of half a tenth (1/20 of full diyah) or more; lesser amounts are paid by the killer from his own wealth. The rationale is to avoid hardship (ihjaf) on the killer — a small sum does not cause hardship. The dividing line is determined by revelation (nass), not reason. The author cites a hadith from Ibn Abbas listing five cases where the 'aqila is not liable: intentional killing, slave's killing, settlement after intentional killing, confession of killing, and less than the arsh of mawdihah (which is half a tenth). The main evidence is the Prophet's ruling that the arsh of a fetus (half a tenth of a man's diyah) is on the 'aqila, implying that anything less is on the killer. The commentary then addresses a potential qiyas: by analogy, either all diyah is on the 'aqila (as Shafi'i holds) or none. The Hanafi school abandons both analogies in favor of the textual evidence (the hadith), establishing the threshold at half a tenth. Amounts below this are treated like ordinary property liabilities, falling on the criminal.

Key Terms
عاقلہ (ʿāqilah): The paternal male relatives (asabah) of the killer who are obliged to pay diyah for unintentional homicide.
نصف عشر (niṣf ʿushr): Half of one-tenth, i.e., one-twentieth (1/20) of the full diyah.
تحکیم (taḥkīm): Arbitration or judge's estimate; here referring to the judge's determination of compensation for injuries.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum amount of diyah that the 'aqila must pay?
A) Any amount, regardless of how small
B) Half a tenth (1/20) of the full diyah or more
C) One-tenth of the full diyah
D) Only the full diyah

Key Takeaway
The 'aqila's liability for diyah in unintentional injuries is limited to half a tenth or more, based on the Prophetic hadith of arsh al-janin.

Cases Where 'Aqila Does Not Bear Diyah
Listing five types of liability not borne by the 'aqila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five cases where the 'aqila is not liable for diyah and identify the exception where they become liable through affirmation.

Core Idea
The 'aqila does not bear diyah for a slave's injury, settlement, or confession unless they voluntarily affirm.

Building on the general rule that the 'aqila bears diyah for unintentional killings, this passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah clarifies important exceptions. These exceptions arise when the obligation stems from a slave's act, a personal settlement, or the killer's own confession—because the 'aqila's mutual support does not extend to these situations. However, if the 'aqila voluntarily affirms such an obligation, they become bound by it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and its basis
Recall that the 'aqila (male agnates) bears diyah for unintentional killings due to the principle of mutual support (nasr). This lesson lists exceptions where that support does not apply.
The general rule is established by the hadith of Ibn Abbas.
2
List the three excluded categories
From the passage, identify: (1) injury caused by a slave, (2) money due from a settlement (sulh) after intentional killing, (3) money due from the killer's confession. These are personal to the killer.
Settlement and confession arise in intentional killings, which are not covered by the 'aqila system.
3
Understand the rationale for each exclusion
A slave does not provide nasr to the 'aqila. Settlement and confession are binding only on the one who makes them because the 'aqila has no authority over such personal obligations.
The 'aqila's authority (wilayah) is limited; they cannot be forced into these agreements.
4
Note the exception: affirmation by the 'aqila
If the 'aqila chooses to affirm (tasdiq) the killer's confession or settlement, they become liable. This is because they have authority over themselves and voluntarily accept the obligation.
The exception shows that the original prohibition was for the 'aqila's own right; they can waive it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولا تعقل العاقلة جناية العبد ولا مالزم بالصلح او باعتراف الجاني لما رويناه ولانه لاتناصر بالعبد والاقرار والصلح لا يلزمان العاقلة لقصور الولاية عنهم قال الا ان يصدقوه لانه ثبت بتصادقهم والامتناع كان لحقهم ولهم ولاية على انفسهم
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور عاقلہ غلام کی جنایت کی دیت ادا نہیں کریں گے اور نہ وہ مال جو صلح سے یا مجرم کے اعتراف سے لازم ہوا ہے اس حدیث کی وجہ سے جس کو ہم روایت کر چکے ہیں اور اس لئے کہ غلام کی وجہ سے ناصر نہیں ہوتا اور اقرار اور صلح عاقلہ پر لازم نہ ہوں گے ان کے اوپر ولایت کے قصور کی وجہ سے قدوری نے فرمایا مگر یہ کہ عاقلہ اس کی تصدیق کر دے کہ وجوب ان کی تصدیق سے ثابت ہوا ہے اور وجوب کا ممتنع ہونا ان کے حق کی وجہ سے تھا اور عاقلہ کو اپنے نفس پر ولایت ہے۔
تشریح یہ وہی باتیں ہیں جو گذشتہ متن میں حدیث کے اندر گذری ہیں جو بالکل سہل ہے۔
He (al-Quduri) said: The 'aqila does not bear the diyah of a slave's injury, nor the property (money) that becomes obligatory by settlement (sulh) or by the criminal's confession (i'tiraf), due to the hadith we narrated and because support (nasr) is not obtained through a slave, and the confession and settlement are not binding on the 'aqila because of deficiency of authority (qusur al-wilayah) over them. He said: Except if they (the 'aqila) affirm him (the killer), because it is established by their mutual affirmation (tasadiq), and the prohibition was due to their right, and they have authority over themselves.
Translation: Al-Quduri said: The 'aqila will not pay the diyah for a slave's injury, nor the property that becomes obligatory by settlement or by the criminal's confession, due to the hadith we narrated and because support is not obtained through a slave, and confession and settlement are not binding on the 'aqila because of deficiency of authority over them. Al-Quduri said: Except if the 'aqila affirms him (i.e., the killer's confession or settlement), because the obligation is established by their mutual affirmation, and the prevention (of obligation) was due to their right, and the 'aqila have authority over themselves.
Explanation: These are the same matters that passed in the previous text within the hadith, which is very clear.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah clarifies additional cases where the 'aqila is not liable for diyah. The matn from Quduri states: (1) The 'aqila does not bear diyah for the injury caused by a slave, because the slave does not provide 'nasr' (support/assistance) — the 'aqila system is based on mutual support among free agnates. (2) The 'aqila is not liable for property that becomes due through a settlement (sulh) after intentional killing or through the killer's own confession, because the 'aqila have no authority to bind themselves in such matters; these are personal obligations of the killer. An exception: if the 'aqila voluntarily affirm and accept the obligation, they become liable because they have authority over themselves. The commentary notes that these rules were already mentioned in the earlier hadith of Ibn Abbas.

Key Terms
صلح (ṣulḥ): Settlement between the victim's family and the killer for monetary compensation in intentional killing.
تصدیق (taṣdīq): Affirmation or admission by the 'aqila of the killer's confession or settlement, making them liable.
عاقلة (ʿāqila): The male agnates (adulthood, sane, free) who collectively bear diyah for unintentional killings committed by their member.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which situation does the 'aqila become liable for a diyah that was initially not binding on them?
A) If the killer confesses
B) If there is a settlement
C) If the 'aqila affirm the confession or settlement
D) If the victim is a slave

Key Takeaway
The 'aqila is not liable for a slave's injury, settlement, or confession unless they voluntarily affirm, because these lack the mutual support that justifies collective responsibility.

Diyah on Confession of Unintentional Killing After Delay
Ruling on diyah for delayed confession of unintentional killing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyah when a killer confesses to unintentional killing after a delay, and explain the qiyas awla reasoning that applies the same deferment as in evidence-based cases.

Core Idea
Delay in bringing the case does not shift liability to the 'aqila; diyah is on the killer's wealth, deferred from judgment day.

This lesson addresses a scenario where the killer confesses to unintentional killing, but the victim's family only brings the case to the qadi after many years. Does the delay affect who pays the diyah? The Hanafi ruling is that the diyah remains on the killer's own wealth, not the 'aqila, and the three-year deferment starts from the date of judgment, not from the killing. The reasoning uses a fortiori analogy (qiyas awla) from the case where the killing is proven by evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The killer confesses (iqrar) to unintentional killing, but the victim's family does not approach the qadi until years later. The text asks: what is the ruling on diyah payment?
The confession is clear; no evidence is presented.
2
State the Ruling
The qadi gives judgment that the diyah is wajib on the killer's own wealth, payable in three years from the day of judgment.
The diyah is not on the 'aqila (tribe) because the delay indicates they do not support the claim.
3
Understand the Reasoning (Qiyas Awla)
The deferment (tajil) over three years is well-established when the killing is proven by evidence (bayyinah). Here, the case is proven by confession, which is stronger. So the same deferment applies a fortiori (awla).
Qiyas awla: if a rule applies in a weaker case, it applies even more in a stronger case.
4
Apply to Similar Cases
Any case where the right is proven by confession (e.g., debt, damage) and the court involvement is delayed, the deferment or timeline starts from judgment, not from the event.
This is consistent with the Hanafi principle that the confessor's liability is immediate but payment can be deferred by judicial discretion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن اقر بقتل خطا ولم يرفعوا الى القاضى الابعد سنين قضى عليه بالدية فى ماله فى ثلاث سنين من يوم يقضى لان التاجيل من وقت القضاء فى الثـ لثابت بالبينة ففى الثابت بالاقرار اولی
ترجمہ اور جس نے قتل خطاء کا اقرار کیا اور انہوں نے مسئلہ قتل کو قاضی کی جانب نہیں اٹھایا مگر چند سالوں کے بعد تو قاتل پر اس کے مال میں دیت کا فیصلہ کیا جائے گا تین سالوں کے اندر اس دن سے جس دن سے فیصلہ کیا جائے اس لئے کہ تا جیل قضاء کے وقت سے ہوتی ہے اس قتل کے اندر جو بینہ سے ثابت ہو پس جو قتل اقرار سے ثابت ہوا ہو اس میں بدرجہ اولی ۔
And whoever confesses to unintentional killing, and they (the victim's family) do not bring it to the qadi until after years, he (the qadi) gives judgment that the diyah is on his (the killer's) wealth, payable in three years from the day of judgment, because the deferment (tajil) is from the time of judgment in the case of what is established by evidence (bayyinah), so a fortiori in what is established by confession (iqrar).
Translation: And whoever confesses to unintentional killing, and they did not raise the matter to the qadi until after years, then the qadi will give judgment that the diyah is on the killer's own wealth, payable within three years from the day the judgment is passed, because the deferment (of payment over three years) is from the time of judgment in the case of killing proven by evidence (bayyinah). So in the case proven by confession, it is even more so (awla).
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah deals with a scenario where the killer confesses to unintentional killing, but the victim's family delays bringing the case to the qadi for years. The ruling: the qadi gives judgment that the diyah is payable from the killer's own wealth (not the 'aqila), because the delay indicates the 'aqila did not support the claim. The payment is deferred over three years from the date of judgment, not from the date of the killing. This deferment (tajil) is established in cases proven by evidence; here, since it is proven by confession, the same deferment applies even more strongly (awla). The logic: when the claim is based on evidence, the court's involvement starts at judgment; confession provides even stronger proof, so the same rule applies.

Key Terms
اقرار (iqrār): Confession of the killer that he committed unintentional killing.
تاجیل (tajīl): Deferment of diyah payment over three years, starting from the date of judgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) From the date of the confession
C) From the date of the qadi's judgment
D) From the date the victim's family knew of the killer

Key Takeaway
The three-year diyah deferment starts from the date of judicial judgment, not from the incident, even when proven by confession after delay.

Diyat — Blood Money

Liability for Blood Money Less Than Half a Tenth
Determining liability for small portions of blood money

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on blood money amounts less than half a tenth and explain the textual evidence (nass) that overrides conflicting analogies.

Core Idea
Amounts less than half a tenth are on the killer, not the 'Aqilah — nass resolves conflicting qiyas.

In Hanafi law, the 'Aqilah normally bears the blood money for accidental killing. However, the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) specified a minimum threshold: one-twentieth of the full diyah for a fetus. This lesson examines amounts below that threshold and why they fall on the killer alone.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the ruling for blood money amounts less than half a tenth? The author states it is placed in the category of property and is obligatory on the killer's own wealth.
This is a concrete legal ruling, not a theoretical discussion.
2
Locate the dalil
The evidence is the hadith where the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) made the 'Aqilah liable for the 'arsh of the fetus, which is one-twentieth of the full diyah (half a tenth).
The hadith is from Sunnah ahad but accepted as reliable in transmission (thubut qati).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Since the hadith fixes the 'Aqilah's liability at exactly half a tenth, any amount less than that is not covered by the hadith. It therefore falls under the general principle of liability for property, which is on the individual.
The analogy is: just as property guarantee is based on valuation, this small amount is treated as personal property obligation.
4
Note the conflicting analogies
Two analogies conflict: one would put all blood money on the 'Aqilah; the other would put none. The author explains that the Hanafi school abandons both analogies and relies on the nass (text).
This shows the hierarchy: nass overrides qiyas when the analogies are in conflict.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text (nass) is decisive: the Prophet's ruling sets a boundary. The mufta bihi position is that amounts less than half a tenth are on the killer's own wealth.
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and consistently relied upon in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المعاقل
پس جو اس سے کم ہو اس کو اموال کے درجہ میں رکھا جائے گا اس لئے کہ یہ (اس سے کم ) حکم کے فیصلہ سے واجب ہو گا جیسے مال کا ضمان تقویم کی وجہ سے واجب ہوتا ہے پس اس وجہ سے وہ ( نصف عشر سے کم ) مجرم کے مال میں ہو گا قیاس پر عمل کرتے ہوئے۔ تشریح نصف عشر سے جو کم ہے وہ عاقلہ پر نہیں بلکہ قاتل کے مال میں واجب ہے۔ کیونکہ قیاس تو یہاں متعارض ہیں ایک قیاس چاہتا ہے کہ قلیل و کثیر کے درمیان فرق کئے بغیر سب دیت عاقلہ پر واجب ہو اور دوسرا قیاس چاہتا ہے کہ عاقلہ پر بالکل واجب نہ ہو بلکہ سب قاتل پر واجب ہو۔ اس لئے ہم نے قیاسوں کو چھوڑ کر نص پر عمل کیا ہے۔ اور نص مذکور سے صاف معلوم ہو گیا کہ نصف عشر سے کم کا ذمہ دار قاتل ہے۔ اور دوسری حدیث یہ بھی ہے کہ نبی کریم صلى الله عليه وسلم نے عاقلہ پر جنین کے ارش کو واجب کیا جو مرد کی دیت کا بلا ہے تو جو مقدار اس سے کم ہو گی اس کو اموال کے درجہ میں رکھا جائے گا کیونکہ جس طرح مال کا ضمان تقویم سے واجب ہوتا ہے اسی طرح نصف عشر سے کم [؟تحکیم] واجب ہوتا ہے لہذا اس کو مجرم کے مال میں واجب کر دیا جائے گا۔
Then whatever is less than it (half a tenth) will be placed in the category of property, because it becomes obligatory by the decree of ruling, just as the guarantee of property becomes obligatory due to valuation. Therefore, that (less than half a tenth) will be in the property of the criminal, acting on analogy. Explanation: What is less than half a tenth is not on the 'Aqilah but is obligatory in the property of the killer. Because the analogies here are conflicting: one analogy wants all blood money to be on the 'Aqilah without distinction between small and large, and another analogy wants none of it to be on the 'Aqilah but all on the killer. Therefore we have abandoned the analogies and acted on the text (nass). And from the mentioned text it is clearly known that the one responsible for less than half a tenth is the killer. And there is also another hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) made the 'Aqilah liable for the 'arsh of the fetus, which is one-twentieth of the blood money of a man, so whatever amount is less than that will be placed in the category of property, because just as the guarantee of property becomes obligatory by valuation, similarly less than half a tenth becomes obligatory by [؟tahkim], therefore it will be made obligatory in the property of the criminal.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof about the liability for blood money amounts less than half a tenth (nisf 'ushr) of the full diyah. The author begins by stating the ruling: such a small amount is treated like property and is therefore on the killer's own wealth, not on the 'Aqilah. The reasoning is that it becomes obligatory by a judicial decree, similar to how a guarantee for property becomes obligatory by valuation. The commentator explains that there are two conflicting analogies: one analogy would put all blood money on the 'Aqilah regardless of amount, and another would put all of it on the killer. To resolve this conflict, the Hanafi school relies on textual evidence (nass) from the hadith where the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) imposed the 'arsh (one-twentieth of diyah) for a fetus on the 'Aqilah. Since that amount is one-twentieth, amounts less than that (less than half a tenth) are not included in the 'Aqilah's liability and fall on the killer. The analogy with property guarantee further supports this: as property guarantee is based on valuation, so too this small amount is treated as property. This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
عاقلہ ('Aqilah): The clan or group of paternal relatives who are responsible for paying blood money (diyah) on behalf of a member who commits accidental killing.
نصف عشر (nisf 'ushr): Half of one-tenth; used here to denote one-twentieth (1/20) of the full blood money, which is the amount of 'arsh for a fetus.
دیت (diyah): Blood money, the financial compensation paid to the heirs of a victim in cases of homicide or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The state treasury (Bayt al-Mal)
D) The victim's family

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses the hadith of the fetus as a textual boundary: half a tenth is the minimum for 'Aqilah liability; anything less is the killer's personal obligation.

Three Exclusions from 'Aqilah Liability
Ruling with proof from al-Quduri's matn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three cases where the 'Aqilah does not bear blood money according to the Hanafi school, and explain the reason for each exception.

Core Idea
The 'Aqilah is not liable for blood money from a slave, a settlement, or a confession unless they affirm it.

The 'Aqilah system in Hanafi fiqh is a collective responsibility for blood money, but it has clear limits. This passage from al-Quduri's matn lists three situations where the 'Aqilah is not responsible, even though the killer may be. Understanding these limits is crucial for correctly assigning financial liability in homicide cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three exceptions
Read the passage and list the three specific cases where al-Quduri states the 'Aqilah does not bear blood money.
These are: slave, settlement (sulh), confession (iqrar).
2
Understand the basis for the slave exception
The text provides two reasons: a hadith (narrated earlier) and the principle that there is no mutual support (tanasur) between the 'Aqilah and a slave. Explain why a slave falls outside the 'Aqilah's circle of responsibility.
The 'Aqilah system is based on tribal solidarity among free Muslims; a slave is not part of that network.
3
Analyze the exceptions for sulh and confession
Note that both sulh and confession are actions by the killer or the victim's family that create an obligation on the killer personally. The 'Aqilah is not bound because they lack authority over these parties. Distinguish these from a judicial ruling based on evidence (bayyinah), which does bind the 'Aqilah.
The key term is 'qusur al-wilayah' (deficiency of authority).
4
Recognize the exception when the 'Aqilah affirm
Al-Quduri adds: 'except if they (the 'Aqilah) acknowledge it.' Understand that if the 'Aqilah themselves accept the obligation (e.g., by affirmatively consenting to the sulh or confession), they become liable because they have authority over themselves.
This is a manifestation of the principle that one can voluntarily undertake obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عاقلہ کن امور کی جنایت کا تحمل نہیں کرتی
قال ولا تعقل العاقلة جناية العبد ولا مالزم بالصلح او باعتراف الجاني لما رويناه ولانه لاتناصر بالعبد والاقرار والصلح لا يلزمان العاقلة لقصور الولاية عنهم قال الا ان يصدقوه لانه ثبت بتصادقهم والامتناع كان لحقهم ولهم ولاية على انفسهم
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور عاقلہ غلام کی جنایت کی دیت ادا نہیں کریں گے اور نہ وہ مال جو صلح سے یا مجرم کے اعتراف سے لازم ہوا ہے اس حدیث کی وجہ سے جس کو ہم روایت کر چکے ہیں اور اس لئے کہ غلام کی وجہ سے ناصر نہیں ہوتا اور اقرار اور صلح عاقلہ پر لازم نہ ہوں گے ان کے اوپر ولایت کے قصور کی وجہ سے قدوری نے فرمایا مگر یہ کہ عاقلہ اس کی تصدیق کر دے کہ وجوب ان کی تصدیق سے ثابت ہوا ہے اور وجوب کا ممتنع ہونا ان کے حق کی وجہ سے تھا اور عاقلہ کو اپنے نفس پر ولایت ہے۔ تشریح یہ وہی باتیں ہیں جو گذشتہ متن میں حدیث کے اندر گذری ہیں جو بالکل سہل ہے۔
What the 'Aqilah does not bear: He (al-Quduri) said: The 'Aqilah does not bear the injury of a slave, nor wealth that becomes obligatory by settlement (sulh) or by the confession of the criminal, because of the hadith we narrated, and because there is no mutual support through a slave, and confession and settlement are not binding on the 'Aqilah due to the deficiency of authority over them. He said: Except if they (the 'Aqilah) acknowledge it, because then it is established by their confirmation, and the prevention (of obligation) was due to their right, and they have authority over themselves. Commentary: These are the same matters that passed in the previous text in the hadith, which is very easy.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from al-Quduri's text, explaining what the 'Aqilah is not responsible for. The 'Aqilah does not pay blood money for injuries caused by a slave, because the slave is not a member of the 'Aqilah's tribe and there is no mutual support with a slave. Similarly, the 'Aqilah is not liable for a debt of blood money that is established by a settlement (sulh) between the criminal and the victim's family, nor by the criminal's own confession. The reason is that these are contractual obligations that arise from the parties' own actions, not from the 'Aqilah's guarantee. The 'Aqilah's liability is based on the principle of mutual support (tanasur) and is triggered only by judicial proof (bayyinah) or a clear ruling. However, if the 'Aqilah themselves affirm and accept the obligation, it becomes binding on them because they have authority over themselves. This is consistent with the earlier hadith mentioned in the text. The ruling is straightforward.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A settlement agreement between the criminal and the victim's family regarding the blood money, often for a smaller amount or different terms.
اقرار (iqrar): Confession by the killer that he caused the death. His confession is binding on himself but not on the 'Aqilah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always
B) Never
C) Only if they themselves affirm it
D) Only if the judge orders it

Key Takeaway
The 'Aqilah is not liable for blood money arising from a slave, a settlement, or a confession, unless they affirm it, because their liability is based on mutual support and judicial proof.

Blood Money on Killer After Confession: Deferment from Judgment Date
Ruling on diyah deferment start date when confession is years later

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when diyah is on the killer's own wealth vs. the 'Aqilah, and apply the rule that the three-year payment period starts from the judge's verdict.

Core Idea
Diyah payment deferment starts from the judgment date, not from the killing.

This lesson clarifies a key point in Hanafi diyah law: the three-year payment period for accidental killing begins at the judge's verdict, not at the time of the incident or confession. Understanding this prevents disputes over delayed cases and ensures the payer receives the full grace period.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Scenarios
The passage distinguishes between killing proven by evidence (bayyinah) and by confession (iqrar). Note who bears the diyah in each case.
Evidence puts diyah on 'Aqilah; confession on the killer's wealth.
2
Determine the Payment Period
In both scenarios, the payment is spread over three years. This is a fixed deferment (ta'jil) granted by law.
The period is three years, not immediate.
3
Identify the Start of the Period
The passage explicitly states the deferment is 'from the day he judges' – i.e., from the date of the judge's verdict, regardless of when the killing occurred.
This is the crucial legal point.
4
Understand the Reasoning (Talil)
The reasoning is qiyas (analogy): since in the case proven by evidence the deferment is from judgment, it is even more so (awla) when proven by confession, because the obligation is established later.
The phrase 'first' (awla) indicates a fortiori reasoning.
5
Apply to a Delayed Confession
If someone confesses years after the killing, the three-year period still starts at the verdict. The delay does not affect the start date.
The clock only starts ticking at judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قتل خطاء کی دیت کس صورت میں عاقلہ پر ہوتی ہے اور کس صورت میں قاتل پر ہوتی ہے
ومن اقر بقتل خطا ولم يرفعوا الى القاضى الابعد سنين قضى عليه بالدية فى ماله فى ثلاث سنين من يوم يقضى لان التاجيل من وقت القضاء فى الثـ لثابت بالبينة ففى الثابت بالاقرار اولی
ترجمہ اور جس نے قتل خطاء کا اقرار کیا اور انہوں نے مسئلہ قتل کو قاضی کی جانب نہیں اٹھایا مگر چند سالوں کے بعد تو قاتل پر اس کے مال میں دیت کا فیصلہ کیا جائے گا تین سالوں کے اندر اس دن سے جس دن سے فیصلہ کیا جائے اس لئے کہ تا جیل قضاء کے وقت سے ہوتی ہے اس قتل کے اندر جو بینہ سے ثابت ہو پس جو قتل اقرار سے ثابت ہوا ہو اس میں بدرجہ اولی ۔ تشریح قتل خطاء میں دیت عاقلہ پر واجب ہوتی ہے جب کہ قتل کا ثبوت بینہ سے ہو اور اگر خود قاتل کے اقرار سے ہو تو دیت قاتل کے مال میں ہوتی ہے اور دونوں صورتوں میں دیت کی ادائیگی تین سالوں میں ہوگی اور مدت کا آغاز قاضی کے فیصلہ کے وقت سے ہو گا لہذا اگر کسی نے قتل کا اقرار کیا یعنی خطاء کا اور چند سالوں تک یہ مقدمہ قاضی کے پاس نہ پہنچ سکا اور کئی سال کے بعد اب یہ مقد مہ قاضی کے یہاں پہنچا ہے تو قاضی نے قاتل کے مال میں دیت کا فیصلہ کر دیا تو اس دیت کی ادائے گی تین سالوں میں ہوگی اور مدت کا آغاز اب فیصلہ کے وقت سے ہوگا نہ کہ قتل کے وقت سے۔
When is the blood money for accidental killing on the 'Aqilah and when on the killer? And whoever confesses to accidental killing and they do not raise it to the judge except after years, then he (the judge) will pass judgment that the blood money is on his (the killer's) wealth, payable in three years from the day he judges, because the deferment is from the time of judgment in (the case) established by evidence, so it is even more so (first) in (the case) established by confession. Translation: And whoever confesses to accidental killing and they did not bring the matter to the judge except after several years, then the judge will rule that the blood money is in his wealth, payable in three years from the day of judgment, because the deferment is from the time of judgment in the killing that is proven by evidence, so in that proven by confession it is even more so. Commentary: In accidental killing, the blood money is obligatory on the 'Aqilah when the killing is proven by evidence (bayyinah), and if it is by the killer's own confession, then the blood money is on the killer's wealth. In both cases, the payment of blood money is over three years, and the period begins from the time of the judge's verdict. Therefore, if someone confessed to accidental killing and the case did not reach the judge for several years, and after some years the case reaches the judge, the judge rules that the blood money is on the killer's wealth, and its payment will be over three years, and the period begins from the time of the verdict, not from the time of the killing.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with its reasoning. It distinguishes between accidental killing proven by evidence (bayyinah) and by confession (iqrar). When proven by evidence, the blood money is on the 'Aqilah; when by confession, it is on the killer's own wealth. In both cases, the payment is deferred over three years. The key point here is the starting point of the three-year period: it begins from the date of the judge's verdict, not from the date of the killing. This is because the deferment is a legal grace period that takes effect only when the obligation is established by a judicial decision. If a killer confesses years later, the three-year term starts from the judgment date, giving him full three years from that point. This is similar to the case proven by evidence, where the deferment is from judgment; it is even more appropriate for confession because the killer's own admission establishes the obligation later.

Key Terms
بینہ (bayyinah): Evidence, typically by two male witnesses, that proves the killing in court.
تا جیل (ta'jil): Deferment or postponement of payment; here, the three-year term for paying blood money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) From the date of the killing
B) From the date of confession
C) From the date of the judge's verdict
D) From the date the victim's family demands

Key Takeaway
The deferment for diyah payment begins at the judge's verdict, not at the time of the killing or confession.

Killer and Guardian's Agreement Not Binding on 'Aqilah
Ruling that agreement of killer and guardian does not obligate the 'Aqilah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the binding effect of an agreement between the killer and guardian on the 'Aqilah, and identify the exception for stipendiary members.

Core Idea
The killer’s confession does not bind the 'Aqilah; only the killer’s own share is due if he is a stipendiary.

This passage addresses a key question in Hanafi diyat law: when the killer and victim's guardian agree that a judge has already ruled for the 'Aqilah to pay, but the 'Aqilah deny it. The ruling clarifies that such agreement is not proof against the 'Aqilah. However, if the killer is a stipendiary (ahl al-ata'), he becomes liable for his own proportionate share.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The killer and the guardian of the victim agree that a judge has already ruled that the 'Aqilah (the killer's tribal group) must pay the diyah. The 'Aqilah themselves deny this.
This is a hypothetical situation presented in the text.
2
Understand the general rule
The text states that such an agreement (tasaduq) is not a proof (hujjah) against the 'Aqilah. Therefore, the 'Aqilah are not obligated to pay anything, even if the killer and guardian agree.
The reason: the agreement is only binding on the parties themselves, not on third parties.
3
Spot the exception for stipendiaries
If the killer has a stipend ('ata') from the state and is registered with the 'Aqilah, then he is considered part of the 'Aqilah. In that case, his own share of the diyah becomes binding on him because his confession is effective against himself.
The exception does not make the entire 'Aqilah liable; only the killer's portion.
4
State the underlying principle
The governing principle is that a person's confession (iqrar) is only valid against himself, not against others. The 'Aqilah's liability is based on mutual support, which is not triggered by the killer's admission.
This principle is foundational in Hanafi law of evidence and obligations.

The killer's confession is not a proof against the 'Aqilah, similarly the agreement of the killer and the guardian of the crime is not a proof. And if the killer and the guardian of the crime agree that the judge of such-and-such city gave judgment that the diyah is on his 'Aqilah in Kufa by evidence, and the 'Aqilah deny both of them, then nothing is on the 'Aqilah because their agreement is not a proof against them, and nothing is on him (the killer) in his wealth because the diyah by their agreement was established on the 'Aqilah by judgment, and their agreement is a proof in their own right, unlike the first. Except if he (the killer) has a stipend ('ata') with them, then it becomes binding on him to the extent of his share, because in respect of his share he is confessing against himself, and in respect of the 'Aqilah he is confessing against them. Commentary: The killer's confession is not a proof against the 'Aqilah, so the agreement of the killer and the guardian is also not a proof against the 'Aqilah. Therefore, if they both agree that the judge of such-and-such city gave judgment that the diyah is on the killer's 'Aqilah with witnesses, and the 'Aqilah deny them, then nothing is obligatory on the 'Aqilah because their agreement is not a proof against them. Yes, if the killer is among the stipendiary people (ahl al-ata'), then since he is like one of the 'Aqilah, so the diyah becomes binding on him to the extent of his share, because a person is taken by his own confession, and his confession is not a proof against others.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a ruling that clarifies a specific scenario: when the killer and the victim's guardian both claim that a judge in another city has already ruled that the 'Aqilah is liable, but the 'Aqilah themselves deny this. The ruling is that the 'Aqilah are not bound by this agreement because it is not a proof against them. The killer is also not personally liable because the agreement asserts that the diyah is on the 'Aqilah by a previous judgment. However, if the killer is a stipendiary (ahl al-ata'), meaning he is a registered member of the 'Aqilah group and receives a stipend ('ata'), then he is considered part of the 'Aqilah. In that case, the killer's own share of the diyah becomes binding on him because his confession is effective against himself, but it does not bind the other members of the 'Aqilah. The reasoning is fundamental: a person's confession is only valid against himself, not against others. The 'Aqilah's liability arises from the principle of mutual support (tanasur), which is not triggered by the killer's admission.

Key Terms
عطاء ('ata'): Stipend or allowance given to soldiers or registered members of the 'Aqilah group from the state treasury.
تصادق (tasaduq): Mutual agreement or confirmation between the killer and the guardian about a previous judgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) If the killer confesses again in court
D) If the judge orders it

Key Takeaway
The 'Aqilah are not bound by an agreement between the killer and guardian; only the stipendiary killer's own share is due.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
8 min
Diyah Liability: Aqila vs Killer's Wealth Based on Mode of Proof
Explains the ruling on diyah liability based on mode of proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two modes of proof for manslaughter and determine who bears liability for diyah, and explain the timeframe for payment.

Core Idea
Diyah is on the aqila if proven by witnesses, but on the killer if proven by his confession.

This passage clarifies a key principle in Hanafi diyat: the party responsible for blood-money depends on how the killing is established. Understanding this distinction prevents injustice – the aqila cannot be held liable based solely on the killer's admission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is 'qatl al-khata'' (manslaughter). The ruling: diyah is wajib (obligatory). Determine whether liability falls on aqila or killer based on proof.
The hukm level is wajib – compensation is obligatory.
2
Distinguish the two modes of proof
Mode 1: Bayyinah (testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two female witnesses) – aqila pays. Mode 2: Iqrar (confession) – killer pays from his own wealth.
Bayyinah establishes the fact against third parties; confession only binds the confessor.
3
Determine the payment period
In both cases, diyah is paid over three years. The period starts from the date of the judge's verdict, not the date of the killing.
This rule protects both parties if litigation is delayed.
4
Note the non-binding effect of confession and agreement
The killer's confession is not proof against the aqila. Likewise, any agreement between the killer and the victim's guardian does not bind the aqila.
The aqila's liability is based solely on external evidence, not admissions or private settlements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشریح قتل خطاء میں دیت عاقلہ پر واجب ہوتی ہے جب کہ قتل کا ثبوت بینہ سے ہو اور اگر خود قاتل کے اقرار سے ہو تو دیت قاتل کے مال میں ہوتی ہے اور دونوں صورتوں میں دیت کی ادائیگی تین سالوں میں ہوگی اور مدت کا آغاز قاضی کے فیصلہ کے وقت سے ہو گالہذا اگر کسی نے قتل کا اقرار کیا یعنی خطاء کا اور چند سالوں تک یہ مقدمہ قاضی کے پاس نہ پہنچ سکا اور کئی سال کے بعد اب یہ مقد مہ قاضی کے یہاں پہنچا ہے تو قاضی نے قاتل کے مال میں دیت کا فیصلہ کر دیا تو اس دیت کی ادائے گی تین سالوں میں ہوگی اور مدت کا آغاز اب فیصلہ کے وقت سے ہوگا نہ کہ قتل کے وقت سے۔ اقرار قاتل عاقلہ پر حجت نہیں اسی طرح قاتل اور ولی جنایت کا اتفاق بھی حجت نہیں
Explanation: In manslaughter, the blood-money is obligatory on the aqila when the killing is proven by evidence, and if by the killer's own confession, then the blood-money is in the killer's wealth. In both cases, payment of the blood-money will be over three years, and the period begins from the time of the judge's verdict. So if someone confessed to manslaughter and the case did not reach the judge for several years, and now the case has reached the judge, and the judge ruled that the blood-money is in the killer's wealth, then its payment will be over three years, and the period begins from now, from the time of the verdict, not from the time of the killing. The killer's confession is not proof against the aqila; similarly, the agreement of the killer and the guardian of the crime is also not proof.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling of diyah in qatl al-khata' (manslaughter). The commentator explains that the method of proof determines who bears liability. If proven by bayyinah (two male witnesses or one male and two female witnesses), the diyah is wajib on the aqila (the killer's paternal relatives). However, if the killer confesses (iqrar), the diyah is from his own wealth. In both scenarios, the payment is spread over three years, starting from the date of the judge's ruling, not the killing date. This ensures fairness if litigation is delayed. The commentator also notes that the killer's confession is not binding on the aqila; likewise, any agreement between the killer and the victim's guardian is not proof against the aqila. This is a foundational Hanafi principle: the aqila's liability is based on external evidence, not on admissions of others.

Key Terms
عاقلہ (al-aqila): The group of paternal relatives (asabah) who collectively bear the diyah in cases of manslaughter.
دیت (diyah): Blood-money paid as compensation for manslaughter or homicide.
بینہ (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two female witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) If proven by evidence, the aqila pays; if by confession, the killer pays.
B) The aqila pays in both cases.
C) The killer always pays regardless of proof.
D) The state pays from the public treasury.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the liability for diyah in manslaughter hinges on the method of proof: witnesses implicate the aqila, confession holds only the killer accountable, with payment spread over three years from the judge's verdict.

When Killer and Wali Agree about Aqila's Liability
Ruling on when killer and wali claim aqila was ordered to pay, but aqila deny

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the liability of the aqila when the killer and victim's guardian mutually agree that a judge ordered the aqila to pay diyah, and identify the exception for a killer who is from ahl al-'ata.

Core Idea
Mutual agreement of killer and wali is not binding on the aqila; only the killer's own share is due if he is from ahl al-'ata.

This lesson addresses a nuanced scenario in Hanafi diyah law. The killer and the victim's guardian both claim that a judge has already ordered the aqila to pay blood-money based on evidence. However, the aqila deny this. The text clarifies that such mutual agreement does not bind the aqila, and the killer is not personally liable unless he is part of the aqila system through receiving a stipend (ahl al-'ata). We will walk through the legal reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
Determine who is making what claim: the killer and wali claim that a judge ruled diyah on the aqila; the aqila deny this.
Focus on the issue of third-party liability.
2
State the legal principle regarding mutual agreement
The principle is that tadaquq (mutual agreement) between two parties is not binding on a third party who is not part of the agreement. The killer and wali cannot, by their agreement, impose liability on the aqila.
This is derived from the general rule that a person’s admission is only binding on himself.
3
Apply to the aqila
Since the aqila deny the claim, their liability is not established by the tadaquq. Therefore, no diyah is due from the aqila.
The aqila are independent; they must be proven liable by independent evidence.
4
Apply to the killer
If the killer is not from ahl al-'ata, he is not liable either because the diyah was claimed on the aqila, not on him personally. His agreement with the wali does not constitute a confession of responsibility.
Contrast this with a case where the killer independently confesses to the crime – then he is liable from his own wealth.
5
Consider the exception for ahl al-'ata
If the killer is registered in the aqila system (receives a stipend), he is treated as part of the aqila. His agreement amounts to an admission against himself regarding his share. Therefore, he must pay that share.
This is because, with respect to his share, he is confessing against himself.

Translation: And if the killer and the guardian both agree that the judge of the city ruled the blood-money on the aqila in Kufa by evidence, and the aqila deny them, then nothing is due from the aqila because their mutual agreement is not proof against them. And nothing is due from the killer's wealth because the blood-money by their agreement is established upon the aqila by the judgment, and their agreement is proof in their own right, unlike the first. Except if the killer has a stipend with them, then he is liable to the extent of his share because he is confessing against himself regarding his share, and against the aqila he is confessing against them. Explanation: The killer's confession is not proof against the aqila, so the mutual agreement of the killer and the guardian is also not proof against the aqila. That is, when the killer himself confessed to manslaughter, then the blood-money is due from the killer's wealth, not from the aqila. So now a question arises: why is the blood-money due from the killer here? The answer is that in the case of confession, he confessed that he killed, so he is held to his confession. But in the present case, the guardian of the victim is claiming the blood-money from the killer and his aqila, and the killer agrees, but the aqila do not, so the blood-money cannot be due from the aqila. Since the killer is one of the aqila, it is not due from him either unless he is from ahl al-'ata, in which case he is treated as a confessor and liable for his share.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: the killer and the victim's guardian both assert that a judge has already ordered the diyah upon the aqila based on evidence, but the aqila deny this. The ruling is that the aqila are not liable because the agreement (tadaquq) between the killer and guardian is not proof against the aqila. Similarly, no diyah is due from the killer's personal wealth because the diyah was supposedly established on the aqila by the judge's ruling. However, if the killer is from ahl al-'ata (those who receive stipends and are part of the aqila system), he must pay his proportional share. This is because, with respect to his own share, his agreement amounts to a confession, which is binding on him alone. The commentary further distinguishes this from a case where the killer confesses to manslaughter independently (without the wali's agreement). In that latter case, the diyah is from his own wealth because his confession is fully binding on him. Here, however, the killer is merely agreeing with the wali against the aqila, not confessing alone. Therefore, he is not liable unless he is part of the aqila through the 'ata system.

Key Terms
تصادق (tadaquq): Mutual agreement or affirmation between two parties, here the killer and the victim's guardian.
عطاء (ata): Stipend given to soldiers or registered members of the aqila system; ahl al-'ata are those entitled to such stipends.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the killer and the victim's guardian agree that a judge ordered the aqila to pay diyah, but the aqila deny this, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi text?
A) The aqila must pay because of the agreement.
B) The aqila do not pay, and the killer does not pay either unless he is from ahl al-'ata.
C) The killer must pay from his own wealth regardless.
D) The guardian must pay.

Key Takeaway
The mutual agreement of the killer and the wali does not bind the aqila; only the killer's own share is due if he belongs to ahl al-'ata.

Free Man Kills Slave by Mistake: Who Pays – Aqila or Killer?
Dispute on liability for accidental killing of a slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on liability for accidentally killing a slave and explain why the value is on the aqila, distinguishing the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The value of a slave accidentally killed is a life-compensation, so it falls on the aqila, not the killer.

This passage addresses a key inter-madhhab dispute regarding liability for accidental homicide of a slave. While both schools agree that compensation is due, they differ on who bears it. The Hanafi school analogizes the value of a slave to the diyah of a free person, placing it on the aqila. Imam al-Shafi'i, in one of his opinions, treats it as property compensation, placing the burden on the killer. Understanding this distinction illuminates the Hanafi principle that compensation for life (badal al-nafs) is always on the aqila.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central issue
What is the legal question being debated? Who must pay the value (qimah) when a free man accidentally kills a slave?
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
The Hanafi school holds that the value is due on the aqila. Their proof: the value is badal al-nafs (compensation for life), and by their established principle, compensation for life is on the aqila.
This principle is derived from the diyah for a free person, which is also on the aqila.
3
State the Shafi'i position and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i (in one of his two sayings) holds that the value is due from the killer's own wealth. His proof: the value is badal al-mal (property compensation), since a slave is property, and property compensation is on the wrongdoer.
The Shafi'i view also allows the value to exceed that of a free person's diyah, but capped at 9990 dirhams.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: under Hanafi, the aqila bears the burden; under Shafi'i, the killer personally pays. This affects who ultimately compensates the slave's owner.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred view)
The Hanafi school relies on its position, treating the value as a life-compensation. The text implies this is the mufta bihi view within the Hanafi school.
No direct refutation of Shafi'i is given; the Hanafi response is implicit in the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال واذا جنى الحر على العبد فقتله خطاً كان على عاقلته قيمته لانه بدل النفس على ماعرف من اصلنا وفي احد قولی اشافعي تجب في ماله لانه بدل المال عنده ولهذا يوجب قيمته بالغة ما بلغت ترجمه قدوری نے فرمایا اور جب کہ آزاد نے غلام پر جنایت کی پس اس کو خطا قتل کر دیا تو قاتل کے عاقلہ پر غلام کی قیمت ہو گی اس لئے کہ قیمت نفس کا بدل ہے اس تفصیل کے مطابق جو ہماری اصل معروف ہے اور شافعی کے دو قولوں میں سے ایک پر قیمت قاتل کے مال میں واجب ہے اس لئے کہ یہ ان کے نزدیک مال کا بدل ہے اور اسی وجہ سے وہ اس کی قیمت کو واجب کرتے ہیں جو بھی ہو جائے ۔ تشریح اگر کسی آزاد نے خطا کسی غلام کوقتل کر دیا تو ہمارے نزدیک قاتل کے عاقلہ پر غلام کی قیمت واجب ہوگی کیونکہ یہ قیمت نفس کا بدل ہے جو خطا قبل کی وجہ سے واجب ہورہی ہے لہذا یہ عاقلہ پر واجب ہوگی کیونکہ بدل نفس کا وجوب عاقلہ پر ہوتا ہے اور امام شافعی اپنے ایک قول میں فرماتے ہیں کہ قیمت عاقلہ پر نہیں بلکہ قاتل کے مال میں واجب ہے کیونکہ ان کے نزدیک یہ قیمت مالیت کا بدل ہے اور مالیت کا بدل مجرم پر واجب ہوا کرتا ہے اور یہی وجہ ہے کہ امام شافعی قیمت کو واجب کرتے ہیں اور فرماتے ہیں کہ قیمت آزاد کے برابر یا اس سے زیادہ ہو جائے تو اتنی قیمت دی جائے گی جو آزاد کی دیت سے دس در اہم کم ہو یعنی ۹۹۹۰ در اہم دئے جائیں گے۔
He said: And if a free man commits an injury upon a slave and kills him by mistake, then upon his aqila is his value, because it is the compensation for the life according to what is known from our principle. And in one of the two sayings of al-Shafi'i, it is obligatory in his wealth because it is compensation for property according to him, and therefore he makes his value obligatory whatever it may be. Translation: Al-Quduri said: And when a free man commits an injury upon a slave and kills him by mistake, then upon the killer's aqila is the value of the slave, because the value is the compensation for the life according to the detail known from our principle. And according to one of the two sayings of al-Shafi'i, the value is obligatory in the killer's wealth because it is compensation for property according to him, and for this reason he makes the value obligatory whatever it may be. Explanation: If a free man accidentally kills a slave, then according to our (Hanafi) school, the value of the slave is obligatory on the killer's aqila, because this value is the compensation for the life which is becoming wajib due to accidental killing; therefore it is on the aqila, because the obligation of compensation for life is on the aqila. Imam al-Shafi'i, in one of his sayings, says that the value is not on the aqila but is wajib in the killer's wealth, because according to him this value is compensation for property value, and compensation for property value is due from the criminal. For this reason, Imam al-Shafi'i makes the value wajib and says that if the value equals or exceeds the diyah of a free person, then such a value will be paid that is ten dirhams less than the diyah of a free person, i.e. 9990 dirhams will be paid.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the liability for accidentally killing a slave. According to the Hanafis, the qimah (value) of the slave is treated as compensation for a life (badal al-nafs) and therefore is due on the aqila, just as diyah for a free person is on the aqila. Imam al-Shafi'i, in one of his two opinions, considers it compensation for property (badal al-mal), since a slave is property; thus it is due from the killer's own wealth, not the aqila. The Shafi'i view also implies that the amount can exceed the diyah of a free person, but they cap it at one less than the full diyah (9990 dirhams). The Hanafi position is that the aqila bears the value because it is a life-compensation, and the amount is limited to the actual value of the slave, which cannot exceed the diyah of a free person.

Key Terms
قیمت (qimah): The market value of the slave, which is paid as compensation for accidental killing of a slave.
عاقله (aqila): The group of male relatives on the father's side who are responsible for paying diyah or compensation in cases of accidental killing.
بدل النفس (badal al-nafs): Compensation for a life, i.e., the blood-money (diyah) or value paid when a life is taken unlawfully.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a free man accidentally kills a slave, who is responsible for paying the value (qimah)?
A) The killer pays from his own wealth.
B) The aqila of the killer pays.
C) The owner of the slave pays.
D) The state pays.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, compensation for accidentally killing a slave is a life-compensation, placing liability on the aqila, not the killer.

Aqila Liability for Injuries Below Life on a Slave
Ruling on compensation for injuries below life on a slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on aqila liability for ma dun al-nafs on a slave, explain the talil (reasoning) distinguishing property from personal injury, and contrast the Shafi'i qiyas.

Core Idea
Injuries below life on a slave are property damages; aqila not liable.

This lesson examines a key inter-madhhab dispute: who must pay compensation when a free person accidentally injures a slave (e.g., cuts off a hand). The Hanafi school treats such injuries as property damages, placing liability solely on the perpetrator. In contrast, one saying of Imam al-Shafi'i extends aqila liability, analogizing to injuries on a free person. We will unpack the reasoning behind each position and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the aqila does not bear compensation for injuries below life (ma dun al-nafs) on a slave. The perpetrator pays from his own wealth.
This is based on the principle that a slave is property, and property damages are not borne by the aqila.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i (in one of his sayings) holds that the aqila does bear such compensation, analogizing to injuries on a free person.
The opponent's qiyas: a free person's injuries below life are borne by aqila, so similarly for a slave.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i qiyas: asl = free person (aqila bears ma dun al-nafs); far' = slave; illa = personal injury? Hanafi rejects the illa.
The Hanafi response: the illa is property status, not personal injury, so the analogy fails.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafi distinguishes: a slave is property (mal), so the rule for property damage applies. Aqila liability is only for loss of life (badal al-nafs) in cases of unintentional killing.
This is consistent with the broader Hanafi principle that aqila covers only talaf al-nafs, not amwal.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Hanafi – the individual perpetrator pays; Shafi'i – the aqila pays collectively.
This affects whether the burden falls on one person or the entire clan/insurance group.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما دون النفس من العبد لا تتحمله العاقلة لانه يسلک به مسلک الاموال عندنا على ماعرف وفي أحد قوليه تتحمله العاقلة كما فى الحر وقدمر من قبل ترجمہ اور غلام کے مادون النفس کے اندر عاقلہ متحمل نہ ہوں گے اس لئے کہ ہمارے نزدیک گذشتہ تفصیل کے مطابق مادون النفس کے ساتھ اموال کا طریقہ اختیار کیا جاتا ہے اور امام شافعی کے ایک قول کے مطابق اس کا تحمل عاقلہ کریں گے جیسے آزاد میں اور یہ ماقبل میں گزر چکا ہے۔ تشریح اگر آزاد نے غلام کوقتل نہ کیا ہو بلکہ کوئی عضو وغیرہ خطا کا نا ہو تو چونکہ ماقبل میں گذر چکا ہے کہ غلام کا مادون النفس اموال کے درجہ میں ہے اور اموال کا متحمل عاقلہ کے ذمہ نہیں ہے اس لئے اس تاوان کا وجوب مجرم پر ہو گا۔ اور امام شافعی کا ایک قول یہ ہے کہ اس کا بھی تحمل عاقلہ کے ذمہ ہے جیسے اگر آزاد کے ساتھ کوئی ایسی جنایت کی جاتی جو مادون انفس سے تو اس کا تحمل عاقلہ کے ذمہ تھا تو یہ بھی عاقلہ کے ذمہ ہوگا۔ پھر مصنف نے فرمایا کہ یہ ماقبل میں گذر چکا ہے۔ اس پر صاحب نتائج الانکار کہتے ہیں کہ یہ حال صحیح نہیں ہے یعنی یہ بحث ماقبل میں نہیں گذری ہے۔
And what is below the life from the slave, the aqila does not bear it, because with it the method of property is adopted according to us, as is known. And in one of his two sayings, the aqila bears it, as in the free person, and it has passed before. Translation: And for injuries less than life on a slave, the aqila will not bear them, because according to us, based on the previous detail, the method of property is adopted for injuries below life. And according to one saying of Imam al-Shafi'i, the aqila will bear it, as in the free person, and this has passed before. Explanation: If a free man did not kill the slave, but caused injury to a limb or something by mistake, then since it has passed before that for a slave, injuries less than life are in the category of property, and the aqila does not bear property damages, therefore the obligation of this compensation is on the criminal. And one saying of Imam al-Shafi'i is that the aqila bears it as well, just as if an injury below life were done to a free person, the aqila would bear it, so here too the aqila would bear it. Then the author said that this has passed before. On this, the author of Nata'ij al-Ifkar says that this statement is not correct, i.e., this discussion has not passed before.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage addresses injuries below the level of life (ma dun al-nafs) inflicted on a slave. According to the Hanafi school, such injuries are treated as property damages (amwal), not as personal injuries, because a slave is property. Therefore, the aqila does not bear the compensation; instead, the perpetrator (the free man) must pay from his own wealth. This is consistent with the principle that the aqila only bears compensation for loss of life (badal al-nafs), not for property damages. Imam al-Shafi'i, in one of his opinions, equates a slave with a free person in this regard, holding the aqila liable for injuries below life as well. The commentator notes that this issue was previously mentioned in the text, but a later commentator (Sahib Nata'ij al-Afkar) disputes that, stating the discussion did not actually appear earlier.

Key Terms
مادون النفس (ma dun al-nafs): Injuries that are less than killing, such as wounds or loss of limbs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a free person accidentally injures a slave (e.g., cuts off a hand), who pays the compensation?
A) The aqila of the free person.
B) The free person himself from his own wealth.
C) The owner of the slave.
D) The state.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between fatal and non-fatal injuries on a slave: only death triggers aqila liability; lesser injuries are property damages borne by the individual perpetrator.
